First Objection: Impossibility of Obligating Knowledge
Taftazani presents the first objection and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection raised against the rational obligation of knowing God and how Taftazani refutes it by distinguishing between two senses of 'the heedless'.

Core Idea
Obligating knowledge is possible because the heedless is not the ignorant but the unreached.

Taftazani has argued that rational obligation to know God is possible. Now he presents a series of objections, the first of which challenges the very possibility of imposing such an obligation. This objection claims that obligating knowledge is either impossible (if directed at the knower) or invalid (if directed at the heedless). Taftazani's response clarifies the critical distinction that saves the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the structure of the objection: it presents two cases – the knower and the other (the heedless). Note the logical terms 'taklīf bi-taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil' and 'taklīf al-ghāfil'.
The objection is a dilemma: either the obligation is for the knower (impossible) or for the heedless (invalid).
2
Understand the answer's key distinction
The answer denies the impossibility by redefining 'heedless' (ghāfil). It explains that the heedless is not someone who lacks knowledge of the concepts, but someone who has not received or understood the divine address. The obligated person already has the necessary concepts (taṣawwur) and is tasked with achieving affirmation (taṣdīq).
This distinction resolves the objection: the obligation is directed at someone who is not entirely ignorant.
3
Connect to the broader argument
See how this specific answer supports Taftazani's overall case for the rational obligation of knowledge. By showing that the obligation is possible, he removes a key obstacle to the proof.
4
Test your understanding
Use the checkpoint question to verify you can identify the definition of 'ghāfil' according to the answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأورد على هذا الاستدلال إشكالات بعضها غير مختص به ولا مفتقر إلى حله، لكونه منعاً على مقدمات مثبتة مقررة، مثل إفادة النظر العلم مطلقاً وفي الإلهيات وبلا معلم وإمكان تحقق الإجماع ونقله، وكونه حجة وبعضها مختص به مفتقر إلى دفعه . وهي خمسة : الأول : أن وجوب المعرفة فرع إمكان إيجابها ، وهو ممنوع لأنه إن كان للعارف كان تكليفاً بتحصيل الحاصل وهو محال، وإن كان لغيره كان تكليفاً للغافل وهو باطل . والجواب أن إمكانه ضروري والسند مدفوع بأن الغافل من لم يبلغه الخطاب أو بلغه ولم يفهمه لا من لم يكن عارفاً بما كلف بمعرفته، وتحقيقه. إن المكلف بمعرفة أن للعالم صانعاً قديماً متصفاً بالعلم والقدرة مثلاً يكون عارفاً بمفهومات هذه الألفاظ، مكلفاً بتحصيل هذا التصديق، وتصور تلك المفهومات، بقدر الطاقة البشرية
And there were brought against this inference objections, some of which are not specific to it and do not require being resolved, because they are objections to established and decided premises, such as: that reflection yields knowledge absolutely, in metaphysics, and without a teacher; the possibility of consensus being realized and transmitted; and its being an argument. And some objections are specific to it and require a rebuttal. They are five: First: That the obligation of knowledge is conditional upon the possibility of imposing it, and that is denied; because if it is for the knower, it would be an obligation to attain what has already been attained, which is impossible; and if it is for other than the knower, it would be an obligation upon the heedless (ghāfil), which is invalid. The answer: That its possibility is necessary, and the basis is rejected because 'the heedless' is one who has not received the address or has received it but not understood it, not one who is not knowledgeable about what he is obligated to know. Its verification: The one obligated to know that the world has an eternal Maker characterized by knowledge and power, for example, is one who knows the concepts of these words; he is obligated to attain this affirmation (taṣdīq) and to conceptualize (taṣawwur) those concepts, to the extent of human capacity.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage presents the first of five specific objections against the rational obligation of knowing God, along with its answer. The author first notes that some objections are not specific to this argument but are general, while others require direct rebuttal. The first objection states that the obligation of knowledge presupposes the possibility of its imposition, which is denied. If the obligation is addressed to the knower, it commands attaining what is already possessed (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), which is impossible. If addressed to another, it commands the heedless (ghāfil), which is invalid. The answer distinguishes two senses of 'heedless': the one who has not received the message or not understood it, as opposed to one who simply lacks knowledge of the specific proposition. The obligated person is already aware of the concepts involved (e.g., 'Maker', 'eternal', 'knowledge', 'power') and is tasked with achieving the affirmation (taṣdīq) and conceptualization (taṣawwur) of these concepts within human capacity. Thus the objection fails by conflating the different meanings of 'heedless'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think obligating knowledge to the heedless is categorically impossible because the heedless is completely ignorant.
Correction: The answer clarifies that the 'heedless' in this context is not one who lacks all knowledge, but one who has not received or understood the divine address. The obligated person already possesses the necessary concepts (taṣawwur) and is required to attain affirmation (taṣdīq), which is possible.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani's answer, who is considered 'the heedless' (al-ghāfil) in the context of the first objection?
A) One who is ignorant of all knowledge.
B) One who has not received the address or has received it but not understood it.
C) One who rejects the truth after it has been explained.
D) One who is born blind.

Key Takeaway
The first objection fails because it misunderstands the 'heedless' as someone devoid of all knowledge, when in fact the obligated person already has the necessary concepts and is only required to achieve affirmation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may have heard the obligation to know God countless times. Do you treat it as an abstract command, or have you paused to examine whether you have truly moved from having concepts about Allah to affirming them with your whole being? What would it take for you to make that shift today?
?
The passage redefines 'heedless' to exclude those who have not received the message. Are you using your knowledge of Islamic concepts as a shield to avoid deeper affirmation? In what area of your faith are you content with mere concepts when Allah demands your heart's full affirmation? Commit to one specific step to move from tasawwur to tasdiq.
?
You have received the address of Islam, and you understand its terms. Yet, is there a gap between what you know and what you truly affirm? The objection says obligating knowledge to the heedless is invalid, but you are not heedless. What is your excuse for not having wholehearted certainty? Resolve to close that gap today.

Impossibility of Obligating Knowledge: Objection and Answer
Al-Taftazani refutes the claim that obligating knowledge is impossible and clarifies the definition of the ghafil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes the objection that obligating knowledge is impossible by redefining the 'heedless' person and distinguishing between conceptualization and assent.

Core Idea
Obligating knowledge is possible because the charge is to obtain assent, not concepts already known.

Al-Taftazani presents the first objection against the obligation of knowledge: if a person already knows, commanding them to obtain what they have is impossible (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil); if they don't know, it's commanding the heedless, which is invalid. He provides a careful answer that clarifies the nature of the charge and redefines the heedless person. This lesson walks through the objection and the powerful response that saves the obligation of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: it claims obligating knowledge is impossible because it either commands the knower to obtain what is already obtained (absurd) or commands the heedless (invalid).
The objection uses two parts: 'if for the knower' and 'if for other than him'.
2
Grasp the answer's key move
The answer first asserts the possibility is self-evident (ḍarūrī). Then it redefines 'heedless' as one who has not received or understood the address, not one who lacks knowledge. This refutes the second horn.
Notice how redefinition saves the obligation without contradiction.
3
See the verification of the charge
The mukallaf already possesses the concepts (taṣawwur) of terms like 'Maker', 'eternal', etc. What is charged is obtaining the assent (taṣdīq) and deeper conceptualization within human capacity. Thus, no taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil occurs.
This distinction between taṣawwur and taṣdīq is foundational in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : أن وجوب المعرفة فرع إمكان إيجابها ، وهو ممنوع لأنه إن كان للعارف كان تكليفاً بتحصيل الحاصل وهو محال، وإن كان لغيره كان تكليفاً للغافل وهو باطل . والجواب أن إمكانه ضروري والسند مدفوع بأن الغافل من لم يبلغه الخطاب أو بلغه ولم يفهمه لا من لم يكن عارفاً بما كلف بمعرفته، وتحقيقه إن المكلف بمعرفة أن للعالم صانعاً قديماً متصفاً بالعلم والقدرة مثلاً يكون عارفاً بمفهومات هذه الألفاظ، مكلفاً بتحصيل هذا التصديق، وتصور تلك المفهومات، بقدر الطاقة البشرية .
The first (objection): That the obligation of knowledge is conditional upon the possibility of its being obligatory, which is denied because if it is for the knower, it would be a charge to obtain what is already obtained, which is impossible; and if it is for other than him, it would be a charge upon the heedless one (al-ghāfil), which is false. The answer: That its possibility is necessary (ḍarūrī), and the support (al-sanad) is repelled by the fact that the heedless one (al-ghāfil) is he whom the address has not reached, or it has reached him but he did not understand it, not he who is not a knower of what he is charged to know. And its verification: Indeed, the one charged with knowing that the world has a Maker, eternal, qualified with knowledge and power, for example, is a knower of the concepts of these expressions, charged with obtaining this assent (taṣdīq) and conceptualizing those concepts, to the extent of human capacity.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against the obligation of knowledge (ma'rifah) and its answer. The objector argues that obligating knowledge is impossible because if the person already knows what is required, then commanding him to obtain it is a charge to obtain what is already obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), which is impossible. If the person does not know, then it is a charge upon the heedless one (taklīf al-ghāfil), which is invalid. The answer first states that the possibility of obligating knowledge is necessary (ḍarūrī), meaning it is self-evident. Then it refutes the definition of 'ghāfil': the heedless one is not someone who lacks knowledge of the required matter, but rather someone who has never received the address or cannot understand it. The one charged (mukallaf) already possesses the concepts of the terms (e.g., 'Maker', 'eternal', 'knowledge', 'power')—this is the conceptualization (taṣawwur) he already has. What he is charged to obtain is the assent (taṣdīq) and a deeper conceptualization to the extent of human capacity. Thus, the charge is not impossible.

Key Terms
معرفة
إمكان
غافل
تكليف
تحصيل الحاصل
تصديق
تصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that commanding the heedless (taklīf al-ghāfil) is always invalid, so obligating knowledge is impossible.
Correction: The answer clarifies that the ghāfil is defined by lack of understanding of the address, not lack of knowledge. The one charged is not heedless in this sense.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, who is considered a 'ghāfil' (heedless) in the context of obligating knowledge?
A) Anyone who does not know what they are required to know
B) Someone who has never received the address or received it but did not understand it
C) Someone who is unaware of the obligation itself
D) Anyone who cannot perform intellectual reasoning

Key Takeaway
The obligation of knowledge is possible because the mukallaf already possesses the conceptual notions and is charged to obtain the assent and deeper conceptualization, avoiding both taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil and taklīf al-ghāfil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know the concepts—'Creator', 'All-Powerful', 'All-Knowing'—but is your heart truly assenting to the reality that this world has such a Maker, or are you merely carrying intellectual baggage? What would change if you obtained that assent today?
?
The passage says you are charged with obtaining assent and deepening conceptualization to the extent of human capacity. In what area of your understanding of God have you failed to deepen—not due to lack of ability, but due to neglect? What step will you take this week to move from conceptual familiarity to living assent?
?
The objection's second horn—charging the heedless—was invalid because the heedless is defined by lack of understanding of the address. Have you ever turned away from the divine address, not because you didn't hear it, but because you chose not to understand? What will you do with the clear address you have just read?

The Second Objection: No Decisive Proof for Obligation
The objector presents two arguments against a decisive proof for obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's argument that there is no decisive scriptural or consensus-based proof for the obligation to acquire theological knowledge.

Core Idea
The objector claims that neither the Quran nor consensus provides a decisive proof for the duty to know.

The second objection against the obligation of knowledge challenges the very foundation of that duty. The objector argues that the Quranic command to know is not unequivocally obligatory in its indication, and that the supposed consensus on this obligation is not transmitted through a decisive chain. This lesson unpacks these two lines of attack.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector states two reasons: the textual proof is not decisive in meaning, and the consensus is not decisive in transmission.
Pay attention to the terms قَطْعِيّ الدَّلَالَة and قَطْعِيّ السَّنَد.
2
Understand the first argument about the text
The verse (فاعلم) uses the imperative, which could be for obligation or recommendation; hence it's not definitive.
3
Understand the second argument about consensus
Consensus on obligation is claimed but transmitted via isolated (āḥād) reports, not tawātur, so it doesn't yield certainty.
The objector also claims a counter-consensus that mere assent suffices, as practiced by the Companions with common people.
4
Reflect on the objection's implications
If the objection holds, the obligation of knowledge would not be firmly established by these proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنا لا نسلم قيام الدليل على وجوب المعرفة، أما النص مثل قوله تعالى : فاعلم أنه لا إله إلا الله) فلأنه ليس بقطعي الدلالة إذ الأمر قد يكون لا للوجوب، وأما الإجماع فلأنه ليس قطعي السند إذ لم ينقل بطريق التواتر، بل غايته الأحاد، فللخصم أن يمنعه بل يدعي الإجماع على أنه يكفي التصديق علماً كان أو ظناً أو تقليداً، فإن الصحابة والتابعين رضي الله تعالى عنهم كانوا يكتفون من العوام بالتقليد الانقياد ، ولا يكلفونهم التحقيق والاستدلال والجواب : أن الظن كاف الوجوب الشرعي على أن الإجماع عليه متواتر إذ بلغ ناقلوه في الكثرة حداً يمتنع تواطؤهم به على الكذب فيفيد القطع، وما ذكر من الإجماع على الاكتفاء بالتقليد فليس كذلك، وإنما هو اكتفاء بالمعرفة الحاصلة الأدلة من الإجمالية، على ما أشير إليه بقوله تعالى : ولئن سألتهم من خلق السموات والأرض ليقولن الله) من غير تلخيص العبارة في ترتيب المقدمات، وتحقيق شرائط الإنتاج وتحرير المطالب بأدلتها، وتقرير الشبه بأجوبتها، على أنه لو ثبت جواز الاكتفاء بالتقليد في حق البعض فهو لا ينافي وجوب المعرفة بالنظر والاستدلال في الجملة. هذا والحق أن المعرفة بدليل إجمالي به يرفع الناظر عن حضيض التقليد فرض عين لا مخرج عنه لأحد من المكلفين، وبدليل تفصيلي يتمكن معه من إزاحة الشبه وإلزام المنكرين، وإرشاد المسترشدين فرض كفاية، لا بد من أن يقوم به البعض.
The second (objection): We do not concede the existence of proof for the obligation of knowledge. As for the textual proof, such as His statement: 'Know that there is no god but Allah,' it is not decisive in indication (qaṭ'ī al-dalāla), because the command may be for something other than obligation. As for consensus, it is not decisive in chain of transmission (qaṭ'ī al-sanad), because it has not been transmitted by tawātur; rather, at most it is aḥād (isolated). So the opponent may deny it, and even claim consensus that it suffices to have assent whether it be knowledge, opinion, or blind imitation (taqlīd). For the Companions and Followers, may Allah be pleased with them, were satisfied from the common people with the imitation of compliance (taqlīd al-inqiyād), and did not charge them with verification (taḥqīq) and inference (istidlāl). And the answer: That opinion (ẓann) is sufficient for legal obligation, and moreover the consensus upon it is mutawātir, for its transmitters reached such a number that collusion upon falsehood is impossible, thus yielding certainty. And what was mentioned of consensus on sufficiency of taqlīd is not like that; rather, it is sufficiency with the knowledge obtained from the general evidence (al-adilla al-ijmāliyya), as indicated by His statement: 'If you ask them who created the heavens and the earth, they will surely say Allah,' without summarizing the expression in arranging premises, verifying conditions of inference, formulating theses with their proofs, and presenting doubts with their answers. Moreover, if it were established that taqlīd is permissible for some, it does not negate the obligation of knowledge by speculative reasoning (naẓar) and inference in general. And the truth: That knowledge by general evidence (dalīl ijmālī) by which the inquirer raises himself from the lowliness of taqlīd is an individual duty (farḍ 'ayn) from which none of the obligated can escape; and by detailed evidence (dalīl tafṣīlī) by which one is able to remove doubts, compel deniers, and guide seekers is a collective duty (farḍ kifāya) that some must undertake.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against the obligation of knowledge and its detailed answer. The objector argues that there is no decisive proof for the obligation. As for scriptural evidence, the verse 'Know that there is no god but Allah' is not decisive in its indication because the imperative form may not denote obligation. As for consensus (ijmā'), it is not decisive in its transmission because it is transmitted via isolated (āḥād) reports, not tawātur. The opponent even claims that there is consensus that mere assent (taṣdīq), whether based on knowledge, opinion, or blind imitation (taqlīd), is sufficient, citing the practice of the Companions and Followers who only required from common people the 'imitation of compliance' without demanding verification and inference. The answer begins by stating that opinion (ẓann) is sufficient for legal obligation, and then argues that the consensus on the obligation to know by inference is in fact mutawātir—its numerous transmitters preclude collusion on falsehood, thus yielding certainty. The supposed consensus on taqlīd is not mutawātir; rather, it refers to a general, non-technical knowledge derived from the general evidence (adilla ijmāliyya) that even common people possess, as the Quranic verse indicates: they instinctively know that God is the Creator. This does not require formal arrangement of premises or handling of doubts. Moreover, even if taqlīd were permissible for some, it does not negate the general obligation of knowledge through reasoning. The passage then concludes with the ruling: knowledge through a general evidence that raises one above taqlīd is an individual duty (farḍ 'ayn) upon everyone; knowledge through detailed evidence enabling one to refute doubts, compel opponents, and guide seekers is a collective duty (farḍ kifāya).

Key Terms
دليل
قطعي الدلالة
قطعي السند
تواتر
آحاد
إجماع
تقليد
ظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Quranic command 'Know that there is no god but Allah' is a clear and unambiguous obligation for everyone.
Correction: The imperative form can indicate other meanings, so it is not decisive (قطعي الدلالة) for obligation without further context.

Checkpoint
According to the objector, why is the consensus on the obligation of knowledge not decisive?
A) Because it contradicts the Quran
B) Because it was transmitted by isolated reports, not tawātur
C) Because the Companions rejected it
D) Because it is based on analogy

Key Takeaway
The objector challenges the obligation of knowledge by arguing that the Quranic proof is not decisive in indication and that the consensus is not decisive in transmission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector claims that the Quranic command 'Know that there is no god but Allah' does not necessarily impose an obligation. When you read that verse, do you feel a personal mandate to seek deeper knowledge, or do you take it as a mere recommendation? What would it mean for your daily practice if you truly treated it as an obligation?
?
The objector says the Companions were satisfied with the common people's blind imitation (تقليد الانقياد). Are you content with accepting your beliefs simply because you inherited them, or have you personally verified the proofs? If tomorrow someone challenged your core beliefs, would your faith stand on mere imitation or on conviction?
?
You now know that the objector argues that the consensus for the obligation to know is not transmitted decisively. Yet the duty to seek knowledge is widely emphasized. How will you respond to this objection in your own heart? Will you seek the decisive proofs, or will you accept the obligation on the basis of overwhelming agreement despite the technicality?

Answer and Ruling: The True Obligation of Knowledge
The answer refutes the objection and provides a nuanced ruling on the obligation of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the response to the objection and the distinction between individual and collective duties regarding theological knowledge.

Core Idea
The answer defends the obligation of knowledge as an individual duty rooted in general evidence and a collective duty in detailed evidence.

In response to the objection, the theologian (al-Taftazani) first argues that opinion (ẓann) is sufficient for legal obligation and that the consensus favoring it is actually mutawātir. Then he clarifies that the alleged consensus on taqlīd really refers to a basic knowledge from general evidence. Finally, he distinguishes between two levels of knowledge: general evidence as an individual farḍ 'ayn, and detailed evidence as a collective farḍ kifāya.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer carefully
The answer begins by asserting that ظن is sufficient for legal obligation, and that the consensus on this is متواتر, hence decisive.
2
Understand the rebuttal of the counter-consensus
The supposed consensus on taqlīd is not a true consensus; it only means that common people are not required to formulate detailed proofs.
3
Grasp the distinction between general and detailed evidence
The passage concludes that knowledge by general evidence (دليل إجمالي) that lifts one above taqlīd is an individual duty (فرض عين), while knowledge by detailed evidence (دليل تفصيلي) for refuting doubts and guiding others is a collective duty (فرض كفاية).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنا لا نسلم قيام الدليل على وجوب المعرفة، أما النص مثل قوله تعالى : فاعلم أنه لا إله إلا الله) فلأنه ليس بقطعي الدلالة إذ الأمر قد يكون لا للوجوب، وأما الإجماع فلأنه ليس قطعي السند إذ لم ينقل بطريق التواتر، بل غايته الأحاد، فللخصم أن يمنعه بل يدعي الإجماع على أنه يكفي التصديق علماً كان أو ظناً أو تقليداً، فإن الصحابة والتابعين رضي الله تعالى عنهم كانوا يكتفون من العوام بالتقليد الانقياد ، ولا يكلفونهم التحقيق والاستدلال والجواب : أن الظن كاف الوجوب الشرعي على أن الإجماع عليه متواتر إذ بلغ ناقلوه في الكثرة حداً يمتنع تواطؤهم به على الكذب فيفيد القطع، وما ذكر من الإجماع على الاكتفاء بالتقليد فليس كذلك، وإنما هو اكتفاء بالمعرفة الحاصلة الأدلة من الإجمالية، على ما أشير إليه بقوله تعالى : ولئن سألتهم من خلق السموات والأرض ليقولن الله) من غير تلخيص العبارة في ترتيب المقدمات، وتحقيق شرائط الإنتاج وتحرير المطالب بأدلتها، وتقرير الشبه بأجوبتها، على أنه لو ثبت جواز الاكتفاء بالتقليد في حق البعض فهو لا ينافي وجوب المعرفة بالنظر والاستدلال في الجملة. هذا والحق أن المعرفة بدليل إجمالي به يرفع الناظر عن حضيض التقليد فرض عين لا مخرج عنه لأحد من المكلفين، وبدليل تفصيلي يتمكن معه من إزاحة الشبه وإلزام المنكرين، وإرشاد المسترشدين فرض كفاية، لا بد من أن يقوم به البعض.
The second (objection): We do not concede the existence of proof for the obligation of knowledge. As for the textual proof, such as His statement: 'Know that there is no god but Allah,' it is not decisive in indication (qaṭ'ī al-dalāla), because the command may be for something other than obligation. As for consensus, it is not decisive in chain of transmission (qaṭ'ī al-sanad), because it has not been transmitted by tawātur; rather, at most it is aḥād (isolated). So the opponent may deny it, and even claim consensus that it suffices to have assent whether it be knowledge, opinion, or blind imitation (taqlīd). For the Companions and Followers, may Allah be pleased with them, were satisfied from the common people with the imitation of compliance (taqlīd al-inqiyād), and did not charge them with verification (taḥqīq) and inference (istidlāl). And the answer: That opinion (ẓann) is sufficient for legal obligation, and moreover the consensus upon it is mutawātir, for its transmitters reached such a number that collusion upon falsehood is impossible, thus yielding certainty. And what was mentioned of consensus on sufficiency of taqlīd is not like that; rather, it is sufficiency with the knowledge obtained from the general evidence (al-adilla al-ijmāliyya), as indicated by His statement: 'If you ask them who created the heavens and the earth, they will surely say Allah,' without summarizing the expression in arranging premises, verifying conditions of inference, formulating theses with their proofs, and presenting doubts with their answers. Moreover, if it were established that taqlīd is permissible for some, it does not negate the obligation of knowledge by speculative reasoning (naẓar) and inference in general. And the truth: That knowledge by general evidence (dalīl ijmālī) by which the inquirer raises himself from the lowliness of taqlīd is an individual duty (farḍ 'ayn) from which none of the obligated can escape; and by detailed evidence (dalīl tafṣīlī) by which one is able to remove doubts, compel deniers, and guide seekers is a collective duty (farḍ kifāya) that some must undertake.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against the obligation of knowledge and its detailed answer. The objector argues that there is no decisive proof for the obligation. As for scriptural evidence, the verse 'Know that there is no god but Allah' is not decisive in its indication because the imperative form may not denote obligation. As for consensus (ijmā'), it is not decisive in its transmission because it is transmitted via isolated (āḥād) reports, not tawātur. The opponent even claims that there is consensus that mere assent (taṣdīq), whether based on knowledge, opinion, or blind imitation (taqlīd), is sufficient, citing the practice of the Companions and Followers who only required from common people the 'imitation of compliance' without demanding verification and inference. The answer begins by stating that opinion (ẓann) is sufficient for legal obligation, and then argues that the consensus on the obligation to know by inference is in fact mutawātir—its numerous transmitters preclude collusion on falsehood, thus yielding certainty. The supposed consensus on taqlīd is not mutawātir; rather, it refers to a general, non-technical knowledge derived from the general evidence (adilla ijmāliyya) that even common people possess, as the Quranic verse indicates: they instinctively know that God is the Creator. This does not require formal arrangement of premises or handling of doubts. Moreover, even if taqlīd were permissible for some, it does not negate the general obligation of knowledge through reasoning. The passage then concludes with the ruling: knowledge through a general evidence that raises one above taqlīd is an individual duty (farḍ 'ayn) upon everyone; knowledge through detailed evidence enabling one to refute doubts, compel opponents, and guide seekers is a collective duty (farḍ kifāya).

Key Terms
ظن
فرض عين
فرض كفاية
دليل إجمالي
دليل تفصيلي
تواتر
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any form of taqlīd (blind imitation) is sufficient for religious obligation.
Correction: The passage shows that only a general knowledge that lifts one above taqlīd is an individual duty, and detailed knowledge is a collective duty; mere taqlīd without understanding is insufficient.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what type of knowledge is a collective duty (farḍ kifāya)?
A) Knowledge obtained through general evidence
B) Knowledge obtained through detailed evidence that enables one to refute doubts
C) Knowledge based on blind imitation
D) Knowledge of revealed texts only

Key Takeaway
Knowledge by general evidence that lifts one above taqlīd is an individual duty, while knowledge by detailed evidence for guiding others is a collective duty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been content with just general, inherited knowledge of your faith, avoiding the hard work of detailed understanding? What would it cost you if you never moved beyond that?
?
If knowledge by detailed evidence is a collective duty that you might be neglecting, what are you doing—or failing to do—to equip yourself to remove doubts and guide others?
?
The passage says general evidence 'lifts you above the depths of taqlīd'. Are you still in that depth? What specific step will you take today to rise above blind imitation?

The Mu'tazilite Proof for Rational Obligation
Mu'tazilite proof for rational obligation via ifham al-anbiya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that the obligation to examine miracles must be rational, or else prophets would be silenced and unable to compel examination.

Core Idea
If obligation were only from revelation, prophets could never start the conversation.

The Mu'tazilites challenged the Ashari position that obligation comes only from revelation by showing it leads to a vicious circle: examination needs obligation, obligation needs the law, the law needs examination. This, they argued, would 'silence the prophets' (ifham al-anbiya) and render prophethood useless. In this lesson, we examine their proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite argument
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It presents the core reasoning: if obligation were only from the Law, a vicious circle would arise. Find the three-step circle: examination depends on obligation, obligation depends on Law, Law depends on examination.
The term 'ifham' (silencing) is key—it means the prophet cannot compel the skeptic to examine.
2
Understand the objection and reply
The passage includes an objection: examination does not depend on obligation. The Mu'tazilites reply that even so, the prophet cannot compel, which is the real meaning of silencing.
Notice how the Mu'tazilites argue that without a prior rational obligation, the prophet has no leverage.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
Map out the argument: premise, consequence (vicious circle), objection, counter-reply. This is a typical kalam disputation format.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجت المعتزلة على أن وجوب النظر في المعجزة والمعرفة وسائر ما يؤدي إلى ثبوت الشرع عقلي، بأنه (٣) لو لم يجب إلا بالشرع لزم إفحام الأنبياء، فلم يكن للبعثة فائدة وبطلانه ظاهر، ووجه اللزوم أن النبي عليه السلام إذا قال للمكلف : انظر في معجزتي حتى يظهر لك صدق دعواي، فله أن يقول : لا انظر ما لم يجب عليّ لأن ترك غير الواجب جائز، ولا يجب علي ما لم يثبت الشرع، لأنه لا وجوب إلا بالشرع ولا يثبت الشرع ما لم أنظر ، لأن ثبوته نظري لا ضروري . فإن قيل : قوله لا أنظر ما لم يجب ليس بصحيح، لأن النظر لا يتوقف على وجوبه . قلنا : نعم إلا أنه لا يكون للنبي حينئذ إلزامه النظر، لأنه لا إلزام على غير الواجب، وهو المعنى بالإفحام وأجيب أولاً بأنه مشترك الإلزام، وحقيقته إلجاء الخصم إلى الاعتراف بنقض دليله إجمالاً، حيث دل على نفي ما هو الحق (٤) عنده في صورة النزاع ، وتقريره أن للمكلف أن يقول لا أنظر مالا يجب، ولا يجب ما لم أنظر لأن وجوبه نظري يفتقر إلى ترتيب المقدمات، وتحقيق أن النظر يفيد العلم مطلقاً، وفي الإلهيات سيما إذا كان طريق الاستدلال ما سبق أنه مقدمة للمعرفة الواجبة مطلقاً، فإن قيل: بل هو من النظريات الجلية التي يتنبه لها العاقل بأدنى التفات أو إصغاء إلى ما يذكره الشارع من المقدمات قلنا : لو سلم فله أن لا يلتفت ولا يصغي فيلزم الإفحام
The Mu'tazilites argued that the obligation to examine the miracle, knowledge, and all that leads to the establishment of the Law is rational, because if it were only obligatory by the Law, it would necessitate the silencing of the prophets, so there would be no benefit in the mission and its falsity is apparent. The aspect of the necessity is that the prophet, peace be upon him, when he says to the obligated person: 'Examine my miracle so that my truthfulness in my claim becomes clear to you,' he can say: 'I will not examine as long as it is not obligatory upon me, because abandoning what is not obligatory is permissible; and it is not obligatory upon me as long as the Law is not established, because there is no obligation except by the Law; and the Law is not established as long as I do not examine, because its establishment is theoretical, not necessary.' If it is said: 'His saying “I will not examine as long as it is not obligatory” is not correct, because examination does not depend on its obligation.' We say: 'Yes, but then the prophet would have no power to compel him to examine, because there is no compulsion regarding what is not obligatory, and that is the meaning of silencing.' And it was answered first: that it is shared compulsion (mushtarak al-ilzam), and its reality is forcing the opponent to acknowledge the violation of his evidence in summary, since it indicates the negation of what is true in his view in the case of dispute. Its explanation is that the obligated person can say: 'I will not examine what is not obligatory, and it is not obligatory as long as I do not examine, because its obligation is theoretical, requiring the arrangement of premises, and the verification that examination yields knowledge absolutely, especially in theology if the method of inference is what was previously mentioned as a premise for the absolutely obligatory knowledge.' If it is said: 'Rather, it is among the clear theoretical matters that the rational person becomes aware of with the least attention or listening to what the Lawgiver mentions of premises.' We say: 'Even if granted, he can choose not to pay attention or listen, so silencing follows.'
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite proof that the obligation to examine miracles and acquire knowledge is rational (not merely from revelation) and the first Ashari response. The Mu'tazilites argue that if such obligation were only from revelation, it would lead to 'silencing the prophets' (ifham al-anbiya), meaning prophets could not compel anyone to examine their miracles. The reasoning: a prophet tells a person to examine his miracle to verify his truthfulness. The person can respond, 'I will not examine unless it is obligatory, and it is not obligatory unless the Law is established (by examination), and the Law cannot be established without examination'—creating a vicious circle. The objection that examination does not depend on obligation is countered: even if so, the prophet would have no power to compel, which is the meaning of silencing. The Ashari respond first with the concept of 'shared compulsion' (mushtarak al-ilzam): this forces the opponent to admit his own argument is self-defeating, because the same reasoning applies to rational obligation as well. The explanation: the obligated person can also say the same about rational obligation (that he will not examine unless it is obligatory, etc.), so the objection applies to both. The Ashari then address a possible objection that the premises are clear theoretical matters requiring minimal attention; they reply that even if so, the person can deliberately ignore, and silencing still follows.

Key Terms
وجوب النظر
إفحام الأنبياء
نظري
ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite argument is that obligation cannot be from revelation at all.
Correction: They argue that obligation must be rational (prior to revelation) to avoid a vicious circle; they do not deny that revelation can also obligate after its establishment.

Checkpoint
What does the Mu'tazilite mean by 'silencing the prophets' (ifham al-anbiya)?
A) Prophets cannot speak clearly.
B) Prophets cannot compel examination because there is no prior obligation.
C) Prophets lose their miracles.
D) The skeptic physically silences the prophet.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite proof shows that if obligation were only from revelation, a skeptic could refuse examination indefinitely, making prophethood ineffective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever delayed acting on a clear call from Allah—like prayer, repentance, or seeking knowledge—by telling yourself, 'I need more proof first'? How does this Mu‘tazilite argument expose that your delay might be an excuse, not a sincere search? What step will you take today to end that pattern?
?
The skeptic in this dialogue refused to examine the prophet’s miracle because he felt no obligation. When you encounter truth, do you wait for a command before you move, or does your conscience already know you must respond? What would it take for you to act on what you already know is right without waiting for a formal obligation?
?
Imagine you are the prophet, and the person you are calling simply refuses to look at your evidence. How would you feel? Now think: do you ever treat Allah’s signs—the Qur’an, nature, your own conscience—with that same cold indifference? Write down one sign you have been ignoring and commit to examining it today.

The Ashari First Response: Shared Compulsion
Ashari first response: mushtarak al-ilzam and its elaboration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ashari concept of 'shared compulsion' (mushtarak al-ilzam) and how it undermines the Mu'tazilite argument by showing it applies equally to rational obligation.

Core Idea
The same vicious circle applies if obligation is rational—the argument is self-defeating.

The Ashari responded to the Mu'tazilite proof with a clever turn: the same reasoning used against revelation-based obligation can be turned against rational obligation. This is 'shared compulsion' (mushtarak al-ilzam): the Mu'tazilite cannot avoid the problem they raised. In this lesson, we see how the Ashari develop this response and address a further objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ashari response
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'وأجيب أولاً'. It explains the concept of shared compulsion and how it forces the opponent to admit their argument is self-defeating.
The core idea: the Mu'tazilite's own reasoning can be used to question rational obligation.
2
Understand the analogy
The Ashari point out that the skeptic can say the exact same thing about rational obligation: examination requires rational obligation, which requires rational proof, which requires examination—another circle.
This shows the Mu'tazilite argument is not decisive.
3
Examine the objection and reply
The passage includes an objection that the premises are 'clear theoretical matters' (nazariyyat jaliyya) needing minimal attention. The Ashari reply: even so, the skeptic can deliberately ignore them, and silencing still follows.
This reinforces that the problem is not solved by labeling premises as clear.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite proof that the obligation to examine miracles and acquire knowledge is rational (not merely from revelation) and the first Ashari response. What is the essence of the Ashari concept of 'shared compulsion' (mushtarak al-ilzam) in response to the Mu'tazilite argument?
A) It shows that rational obligation also leads to the same problem, so the argument is self-defeating.
B) It proves that obligation is purely from revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When has your own argument been turned back on you, and how did you respond? Do you see the Ashari's point about shared compulsion as a way to humble your own certainty?
?
The skeptic can refuse to listen even to clear premises. What areas of your faith or practice are you avoiding because you don't want to pay attention? What would it cost you to truly examine them?
?
The vicious circle arises from demanding a reason for obligation before fulfilling it. Is there an obligation in your life you are delaying because you want to understand it fully first? How might that delay reflect a lack of trust in Allah's command?

The Second Ashari Response: Distinguishing Actual Obligation from Knowledge
Ashari solution distinguishing actual obligation from knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ashari distinction between actual obligation and knowledge of obligation, and why conflating them leads to logical error.

Core Idea
Obligation exists in reality, independent of our knowledge of it.

In this passage, the Ashari theologian presents a precise logical solution (al-hall) to the Mu'tazilite argument. He identifies the key error: conflating the actual existence of obligation and divine law with one's knowledge of them. This distinction resolves the apparent circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the distinction between 'fi nafs al-amr' and 'ilm'.
This passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three interpretations
The passage analyzes three possible meanings of the Mu'tazilite's statements. Find each one and see why the Ashari deems it flawed.
Write them down in your own words.
3
Understand the correct dependency
The passage states that knowledge of obligation depends on obligation, not the reverse. Reflect on why this avoids ignorance.
This is the core of the solution.
4
Connect to the fallacy
The Mu'tazilite's error is assuming that obligation depends on knowledge. This leads to an infinite regress or circularity.
The solution shows the error is a category mistake.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانياً : بالحل وهو تعيين موضع الغلط، وذلك أن صحة إلزامه النظر إنما تتوقف على وجوب النظر، وثبوت الشرع في نفس الأمر، لا على علمه بذلك، والمتوقف على النظر هو علمه بذلك، لا تحققهما في نفس الأمر، فهو إن أراد نفس الوجوب والثبوت لم يصح قوله لا يثبت الشرع ما لم أنظر، وإن أراد العلم بهما لم يصح قوله لا أنظر ما لم يجب ، وإن أراد (١) من الوجوب التحقق، وفي الثبوت العلم به لم يصح قوله لا يجب عليّ ما لم يثبت الشرع، لأن الوجوب عليه لا يتوقف على العلم بالوجوب، ليلزم توقفه على العلم بثبوت الشرع، بل العلم بالوجوب يتوقف على الوجوب لئلا يكون جهلاً، وهذا ما قال في المواقف : إن قولك لا يجب علي ما لم يثبت الشرع . قلنا : إن هذا القول إنما يصح لو كان الوجوب عليه موقوفاً على العلم بالوجوب . فقوله قلنا . .. الخ، خبر إن والعائد اسم الإشارة، وإن خص إرادة العلم بقوله لا يثبت الشرع ما لم أنظر ، وإرادة التحقق بقوله لا أنظر ما لم يجب، صحت المقدمات لكن تختل (٢) صورة القياس لعدم تكرر الوسط ، فهذا قياس صحة مادته في فساد صورته وبالعكس.
And secondly: by the solution (al-hall), which is identifying the place of error. That is because the validity of compelling him to examine depends only on the obligation of examination and the establishment of the Law in reality (fi nafs al-amr), not on his knowledge of that. And what depends on examination is his knowledge of that, not their actual occurrence in reality. So if he intends the actual obligation and establishment, his statement 'the Law is not established unless I examine' is not correct. And if he intends knowledge of them, his statement 'I will not examine unless it is obligatory' is not correct. And if he intends from obligation the actual occurrence, and from establishment knowledge of it, his statement 'it is not obligatory upon me unless the Law is established' is not correct, because the obligation upon him does not depend on knowledge of the obligation, so that it would depend on knowledge of the establishment of the Law. Rather, knowledge of the obligation depends on the obligation, so as not to be ignorance. And this is what was said in al-Mawaqif: 'Your statement “it is not obligatory upon me unless the Law is established” – we say: this statement is only correct if the obligation upon him were dependent on knowledge of the obligation.' So his statement 'we say...' etc., the predicate of 'inna' and the referent of the demonstrative pronoun. And if he specifically intends knowledge by his statement 'the Law is not established unless I examine', and actual occurrence by his statement 'I will not examine unless it is obligatory', the premises are correct but the form of the syllogism becomes corrupt due to the non-repetition of the middle term. So this is a syllogism whose matter is sound but form is corrupt, and vice versa.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents the second Ashari response, called 'the solution' (al-hall), which precisely identifies the logical error in the Mu'tazilite argument. The key distinction is between the actual existence of obligation and the Law in reality (fi nafs al-amr) and one's knowledge of them. The validity of compelling someone to examine depends on the actual obligation and establishment, not on the person's knowledge of them. Conversely, what depends on examination is the person's knowledge, not the actual facts. The Ashari then analyzes three possible interpretations of the Mu'tazilite's statements: If the Mu'tazilite means actual obligation and establishment, then his claim 'the Law is not established unless I examine' is false because the Law is established independently of his examination. If he means knowledge of them, then his claim 'I will not examine unless it is obligatory' is false because the obligation exists independently of his knowledge. If he mixes – intending actual occurrence for obligation and knowledge for establishment – then his statement 'it is not obligatory upon me unless the Law is established' is false because the obligation does not depend on knowledge. The correct relation is that knowledge of obligation depends on obligation (to avoid ignorance), not the reverse. The passage then references al-Mawaqif, affirming that the objection only stands if obligation depended on knowledge. Finally, it notes that if the Mu'tazilite intends knowledge for the establishment clause and actual occurrence for the examination clause, then the premises are true but the syllogism's form is invalid due to non-repetition of the middle term (a logical fallacy), resulting in a syllogism with sound matter but corrupt form, or vice versa.

Key Terms
الحل (the solution)
نفس الأمر (in reality)
صحة الإلزام (validity of compulsion)
العلم بالوجوب (knowledge of obligation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ashari solution denies the necessity of rational obligation altogether.
Correction: The Ashari solution does not deny obligation; it clarifies that the obligation to examine depends on actual obligation, not on one's knowledge of it.

Checkpoint
According to the Ashari solution, what does the validity of compelling someone to examine depend on?
A) The person's knowledge of the obligation.
B) The actual obligation and establishment of the Law in reality.
C) The person's examination itself.
D) The repetition of the middle term.

Key Takeaway
The Ashari solution shows that the validity of compulsion depends on actual obligation, not on knowledge, thereby resolving the logical fallacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you living as though your obligations to God depend on your knowledge and convenience, or as though they are real whether you acknowledge them or not? What would change today if you truly believed that? Commit to one action that reflects this belief.
?
When you neglect an act of worship, do you excuse yourself by saying 'I didn't know it was obligatory' when deep down you know it is? How does this lesson challenge that excuse? What will you do about it?
?
The passage says knowledge of obligation depends on the obligation, not the reverse. In what area of your life are you waiting for certainty before acting? Stop delaying—choose one thing you know is required of you and do it before the day ends.

The Syllogistic Fallacy: Non-Repetition of the Middle Term
Logical analysis of syllogistic form in the Ashari response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how mixing premises with different intended meanings leads to a syllogistic fallacy due to non-repetition of the middle term.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism requires a consistent middle term.

This passage concludes the second Ashari response by examining the Mu'tazilite argument from a logical perspective. Even if the premises are individually true, if the middle term (the repeated term) is not consistent, the syllogism becomes formally invalid. This highlights the importance of logical form in theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the syllogism
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the conditional statement about mixing intentions.
This part references al-Mawaqif.
2
Understand the condition
The passage says if the Mu'tazilite intends knowledge for one premise and actual occurrence for the other, the premises are true but the form is corrupt due to non-repetition of the middle term.
The middle term is the term that appears in both premises.
3
Reflect on the significance
This shows that even correct premises can lead to an invalid conclusion if the syllogistic form is flawed.
It underscores the need for both true premises and valid form.

Source Text

Commentary

Even if premises are true, a syllogism fails if the middle term is not repeated consistently.

?

?

Resolving the Fallacy: Real Obligation vs. Knowledge
The author resolves the opponent's fallacy by distinguishing between real obligation and knowledge of it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to resolve the objection against the obligation of reflection by carefully distinguishing between actual obligation and knowledge of that obligation.

Core Idea
Obligation does not depend on knowing it; knowledge depends on obligation.

This passage presents the second refutation of an opponent who claims that reflection cannot be obligatory unless the Law is established. The author shows that the opponent's argument is built on a confusion between the real existence of obligation and our epistemic awareness of it. By disentangling these two levels, the author dismantles the apparent circularity and clarifies the correct logical relationship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the structure: the author first restates the objection, then offers a concession, then gives the main resolution.
The text includes a brief note from al-Mawaqif; notice how it supports the argument.
2
Identify the opponent's three possible meanings
The author examines the opponent's statement 'It is not obligatory unless I reflect' by interpreting 'obligation' and 'establishment of the Law' in three ways: as real entities, as knowledge of them, or as a mix. Try to find these three cases in the text.
3
Understand why each interpretation fails
For each of the three meanings, the author shows why the opponent's claim is invalid. Focus on the key point: obligation does not depend on knowledge, but knowledge depends on obligation.
This is the core of the resolution.
4
Examine the syllogistic note
At the end, the author notes that if the opponent adjusts the premises to avoid ambiguity, the syllogism becomes formally invalid due to a missing middle term. Reflect on why logical form matters in such arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يجب ما لم أنظر لأن وجوبه نظري يفتقر إلى ترتيب المقدمات، وتحقيق أن النظر يفيد العلم مطلقاً، وفي الإلهيات سيما إذا كان طريق الاستدلال ما سبق أنه مقدمة للمعرفة الواجبة مطلقاً، فإن قيل: بل هو من النظريات الجلية التي يتنبه لها العاقل بأدنى التفات أو إصغاء إلى ما يذكره الشارع من المقدمات قلنا: لو سلم فله أن لا يلتفت ولا يصغي فيلزم الإفحام . من وثانياً: بالحل وهو تعيين موضع الغلط، وذلك أن صحة إلزامه النظر إنما تتوقف على وجوب النظر، وثبوت الشرع في نفس الأمر، لا على علمه بذلك، والمتوقف على النظر هو علمه بذلك، لا تحققهما في نفس الأمر، فهو إن أراد نفس الوجوب والثبوت لم يصح قوله لا يثبت الشرع ما لم أنظر، وإن أراد العلم بهما لم يصح قوله لا أنظر ما لم يجب، وإن أراد (۱) من الوجوب التحقق، وفي الثبوت العلم به لم يصح قوله لا يجب عليّ ما لم يثبت الشرع، لأن الوجوب عليه لا يتوقف على العلم بالوجوب، ليلزم توقفه على العلم بثبوت الشرع، بل العلم بالوجوب يتوقف على الوجوب لئلا يكون جهلاً، وهذا ما قال في المواقف: إن قولك لا يجب علي ما لم يثبت الشرع . قلنا: إن هذا القول إنما يصح لو كان الوجوب عليه موقوفاً على العلم بالوجوب . فقوله قلنا . . . الخ، خبر إن والعائد اسم الإشارة، وإن خص إرادة العلم بقوله لا يثبت الشرع ما لم أنظر، وإرادة التحقق بقوله لا أنظر ما لم يجب، صحت المقدمات لكن تختل (۲) صورة القياس لعدم تكرر الوسط، فهذا قياس صحة مادته في فساد صورته وبالعكس.
And it is not obligatory unless I reflect, because its obligation is theoretical, requiring the arrangement of premises, and the verification that reflection yields knowledge absolutely, and in divine matters especially if the method of inference is what was mentioned earlier as a premise for obligatory knowledge absolutely. If it is said: Rather it is among the evident theoretical matters that the intelligent person realizes with the least attention or listening to what the Lawgiver mentions of premises, we say: Even if conceded, he may not pay attention nor listen, and thus an impasse results. And secondly: By resolution, which is identifying the place of error. That is, the validity of imposing reflection on him depends on the obligation of reflection and the establishment of the Law in reality, not on his knowledge of that. What depends on reflection is his knowledge of that, not the actuality of both in reality. So if he means the actual obligation and establishment, his statement 'The Law is not established unless I reflect' is invalid. If he means knowledge of them, his statement 'I do not reflect unless it is obligatory' is invalid. If he means (1) from obligation the actuality and from establishment knowledge of it, his statement 'It is not obligatory upon me unless the Law is established' is invalid, because obligation upon him does not depend on knowledge of obligation, so that it would entail dependence on knowledge of establishment of the Law. Rather, knowledge of obligation depends on obligation to avoid ignorance. And this is what was said in al-Mawaqif: Your statement 'It is not obligatory upon me unless the Law is established.' We say: This statement is only valid if obligation upon him were dependent on knowledge of obligation. So his statement 'We say...' etc., is the predicate of 'inna' and the referent is the demonstrative pronoun. And if he particularizes the intention of knowledge by his statement 'The Law is not established unless I reflect' and the intention of actuality by his statement 'I do not reflect unless it is obligatory', the premises are valid but the form of the syllogism is corrupted due to the non-repetition of the middle term. So this is a syllogism sound in matter but corrupt in form, and vice versa.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection regarding the obligation of reflection (nazar). The author provides a second answer (by resolution) to the opponent's claim that reflection is not obligatory unless the Law is established. He begins by restating the objection: the opponent says reflection is not obligatory unless he reflects, because its obligation is theoretical. Then he considers a potential objection that reflection is evident and the intelligent person can realize it with minimal attention. The author responds that even if conceded, the person may choose not to pay attention, leading to an impasse. The main resolution follows: the author identifies the fallacy by distinguishing between the real obligation and establishment of the Law and the knowledge of them. He argues that the opponent's argument is ambiguous. If the opponent means the real obligation and establishment, then his statement is invalid because reflection does not depend on knowledge. If he means knowledge of them, then his statement is invalid because reflection is not dependent on that knowledge either. If he mixes the two, his statement is still invalid. The author explains that obligation does not depend on knowledge of obligation; rather, knowledge depends on obligation. He cites al-Mawaqif in support. Finally, he notes that if the opponent adjusts his premises to avoid ambiguity, the syllogism becomes invalid due to non-repetition of the middle term, thus the syllogism is either sound in matter but corrupt in form or vice versa.

Key Terms
النظر
الوجوب
ثبوت الشرع
الحل
تعيين موضع الغلط
القياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent believes that the obligation of reflection depends on one's knowledge of the obligation, creating a vicious circle.
Correction: The obligation of reflection is real and independent; knowledge of the obligation depends on the obligation, not the other way around.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct relation between the obligation of reflection and knowledge of that obligation?
A) Knowledge of obligation depends on the obligation itself.
B) The obligation depends on knowledge of obligation.
C) They are independent of each other.
D) Neither exists without the other.

Key Takeaway
The obligation of reflection is real and does not depend on our knowledge of it; rather, our knowledge depends on the real obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have I ever excused myself from a clear obligation by claiming I wasn't sure about it? What would it mean for my soul to admit that the obligation is real even when I ignore it?
?
When I use the phrase 'I need to be convinced first' before acting on a religious duty, am I really waiting for evidence, or am I hiding behind a philosophical fallacy to avoid personal responsibility?
?
If my knowledge of an obligation depends on my own effort of reflection, and that reflection itself is obligatory, then what is my honest excuse for not even beginning the search? Today, what one step will I take to confront this obligation directly?

Refuting the Obligation of Doubt as a Premise for Knowledge
Al-Taftazani refutes two weak arguments regarding the obligation of doubt as a premise for nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the obligation of doubt as a premise for nazar or knowledge is considered weak in kalam reasoning, and how objections about illusion, assumption, taqlid, and compound ignorance are resolved.

Core Idea
Doubt is not itself obligatory; it is only a rational means to nazar.

Al-Taftazani examines the claim that doubt is obligatory as a prerequisite for nazar (rational inquiry). He refutes two attempts to ground this obligation—one based on it being a premise for knowledge, another based on it being a premise for nazar. Along the way, he clarifies what it means for something to be a 'premise of the obligatory' and addresses objections about cases where doubt is absent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two weak positions
Read the opening lines to recognize that the passage targets two views: (1) doubt is obligatory because it is a premise for knowledge, and (2) doubt is obligatory because it is a premise for nazar. The author states both are weak.
2
Understand the first refutation
Focus on the paragraph beginning with 'As for the first'. The author argues that the proponents do not mean by 'premise of the obligatory' a voluntary act, but rather the ability to acquire it (like purity for prayer). The obligation of a premise is the obligation to acquire it, which does not apply to doubt.
3
Understand the second refutation and follow-up
Examine the second objection: if doubt were obligatory, then nazar would not be required in cases of illusion, assumption, taqlid, or compound ignorance. The author shows these are repelled by showing they fall under doubt or require nazar to overcome them. Finally, note the objection about pure doubt not being a cause of nazar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للمقيد به كالنصاب للزكاة والاستطاعة للحج، فلا يجب تحصيله، ولما كان (۱) إيجاب المعرفة هو إيجاب النظر . قال في المواقف : إن وجوب المعرفة مقيد بالشك، وإلا فالقول بوجوب الشك إنما يبنى (۲) على كونه مقدمة للنظر، لا للمعرفة وكلا الوجهين ضعيف .
أما الأول : فلأنهم لا يعنون بمقدورية مقدمة الواجب أن يكون من الأفعال الاختيارية، بل أن يتمكن المكلف من تحصيله كالطهارة للصلاة وملك النصاب للزكاة، ومعنى ،وجوبها، وجوب تحصيلها.
وأما الثاني : فلأنه يقتضي أن لا يجب النظر والمعرفة عند الوهم، أو الظن، أو التقليد أو الجهل المركب وفساده ،بين ويمكن دفع الوهم، والظن بأن الشك الشك يتناولهما لأن معناه التردد في النسبة ، (۳) إما على استواء وهـو المحض أو رجحان لأحد الجانبين وهو الظن والوهم، ودفع التقليد، والجهل المركب بأن الواجب معها هو النظر في الدليل، ومعرفة وجـه دلالته ليؤلا إلى العلم ، وذلك لأن امتناع النظر والطلب عند الجزم بالمطلوب أو نقيضه مما لم يقع فيه نزاع، وقد يقال في رد الشك المحض (٤) أنه وإن كان مقدمة للنظر الواجب فليس من أسبابه ليكون إيجابه إيجاباً له بمعنى تعلق خطاب الشارع به وفيه نظر، لأن مراد أبي هاشم هو الوجوب العقلي كالنظر والمعرفة . نعم لو قيل : إنه ليس من المعاني التي يطلبها العاقل، ويحكم باستحقاق تاركه الذم لكان شيئاً . وستعرف فساد النظر بمعرفة معنى الوجوب العقلي (٥) .
For the restricted (obligation) like the nisab for zakat and the ability for hajj, so it is not obligatory to acquire it. And since the obligation of knowledge is the obligation of nazar, he said in al-Mawaqif: Indeed the obligation of knowledge is restricted by doubt, otherwise the statement of the obligation of doubt is based on its being a premise for nazar, not for knowledge. And both aspects are weak. As for the first: Because they do not mean by 'being a premise of the obligatory' that it be among voluntary acts, but rather that the responsible person is able to acquire it, like purity for prayer and ownership of nisab for zakat, and the meaning of its obligation is the obligation of its acquisition. As for the second: Because it requires that nazar and knowledge not be obligatory in case of illusion, or assumption, or taqlid, or compound ignorance; its corruption is clear. And it is possible to repel illusion and assumption by (saying) that doubt includes both of them because its meaning is hesitation regarding the relation, either equally which is pure doubt, or preponderance of one side which is assumption and illusion. And to repel taqlid and compound ignorance by (saying) that the obligatory with them is nazar in the evidence and knowledge of its implication so that it leads to knowledge. That is because refraining from nazar and seeking in case of certainty of the sought or its opposite is not subject to dispute. And it may be said in refutation of pure doubt that even if it is a premise for the obligatory nazar, it is not among its causes so that making it obligatory would be an obligation of it in the sense of the address of the Lawgiver applying to it—in this is consideration, because Abu Hashim's intent is rational obligation like nazar and knowledge. Yes, if it were said: It is not among the meanings that the rational person seeks, and that he judges the abandoner deserving blame, it would be something. And you will know the corruption of nazar by knowing the meaning of rational obligation.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two weak positions regarding the obligation of doubt as a prerequisite for nazar. The author begins by discussing the view that the obligation of knowledge is the obligation of nazar, and he relates that in al-Mawaqif, the obligation of knowledge is said to be restricted by doubt. The claim that doubt itself is obligatory is built on it being a premise for nazar, not for knowledge. The author states that both ways of understanding this are weak. He then explains the first weakness: The claim's proponents do not mean by 'being a premise of the obligatory' that it is a voluntary act; rather, they mean that the mukallaf is able to acquire it, like purity for prayer or owning the nisab for zakat. The obligation of such a premise is the obligation to acquire it. The second weakness is that this view would entail that nazar and knowledge are not obligatory in cases of illusion, assumption, taqlid, or compound ignorance, which is clearly corrupt. The author then shows how these cases can be repelled: illusion and assumption fall under doubt because doubt is hesitation—either equal (pure doubt) or preponderant (assumption/illusion). Taqlid and compound ignorance are repelled by saying that the obligatory with them is nazar in the evidence and understanding its import, so that it leads to knowledge. The author then mentions a further objection: pure doubt, though a premise for obligatory nazar, is not a cause, so its obligation does not mean the Lawgiver addresses it directly. The author notes that Abu Hashim intended rational obligation, not shar'i. Finally, he remarks that if one were to say doubt is not among the meanings that the rational person seeks and that the deserver of blame is the one who abandons it, that would be something, and he promises that the corruption of nazar will be known through understanding the meaning of rational obligation.

Key Terms
نظر
شك
وجوب عقلي
مقدمة الواجب
إيجاب المعرفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the obligation of doubt is a shar'i obligation like other duties, and that it is directly commanded by the Lawgiver.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the obligation of doubt is rational (aqlī) and only as a means to nazar, not a direct command from the Lawgiver. The obligation of a premise is the obligation to acquire it, and doubt is not a voluntary act that can be commanded.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the first view (that the obligation of doubt is based on its being a premise for knowledge) considered weak?
A) Because doubt is not a voluntary act and cannot be commanded.
B) Because the proponents mean by 'premise of the obligatory' not voluntary acts but the ability to acquire it, and the obligation of doubt is not the same as the obligation of knowledge.
C) Because doubt is not a cause of nazar.
D) Because the Lawgiver does not address the one in a state of doubt.

Key Takeaway
Doubt is a rational prerequisite for nazar, but it is not itself obligatory in the same way that nazar is; the obligation of a premise is the obligation to acquire it, and doubt cannot be directly commanded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When faced with ambiguity in matters of faith, do you instinctively retreat into comfortable taqlid or do you engage in the difficult work of nazar? What is holding you back from seeking evidence directly?
?
Are there areas of your belief where you have never allowed yourself to experience true doubt—not as a crisis but as a honest questioning that leads to deeper certainty? What would it cost you to enter that space of inquiry?
?
Have you ever mistaken a mere mental state (like feeling doubtful) for a virtue, assuming that uncertainty itself pleases Allah? How can you redirect your energy from passively doubting to actively seeking proofs as this lesson demands?

Proofs for Attaining Assent: Syllogism, Induction, and Analogy
Definition and classification of proofs for tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of proof (dalil) in the context of attaining assent (tasdiq) and be able to identify its three types: syllogism, induction, and analogy.

Core Idea
Proofs are the instruments of assent, guided by suitability between argument and conclusion.

This lesson introduces the concept of proof (dalil) as the means to reach certain assent. The author explains why it is called 'proof' and 'argument' and then enumerates three types: syllogism, induction, and analogy. A key condition is the existence of suitability (munasaba) between the evidence and the conclusion. Let's examine the passage.

Guided Path
1
Understand the terminology
Notice that the passage gives two names for the means of assent: dalil (proof) and hujja (argument). Each name highlights a different aspect: guidance and victory.
2
Identify the three types
The passage lists qiyas (syllogism), istiqra' (induction), and tamthil (analogy). These are the three logical methods for deriving new knowledge from existing premises.
3
Reflect on the condition of suitability
The passage states that there must be a munasaba (suitability) between the argument and the sought conclusion. This is crucial for the inference to be valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والموصل إلى التصديق ويسمى الدليل لما فيه من الإرشاد إلى المطلوب والحجة لما في التمسك به من الغلبة على الخصم إما قياس، وإما استقراء، وإما تمثيل، إذ لا بد من مناسبة بين الحجة والمطلوب ليمكن استفادته
And the means to assent (tasdiq) is called the proof (dalil) because of the guidance it contains to the sought thing, and the argument (hujja) because of the victory over the opponent in adhering to it. It is either a syllogism (qiyas), or an induction (istiqra'), or an analogy (tamthil), for there must be a suitability between the argument and the sought so that it can be derived.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage introduces the means to attain assent (tasdiq), which is called the proof (dalil) because it guides to the sought, and also called the argument (hujja) because it provides victory over the opponent. It enumerates three types: syllogism (qiyas), induction (istiqra'), and analogy (tamthil). The condition is that there must be a suitability (munasaba) between the argument and the sought, so that the sought can be inferred from the argument. The passage is cut off and likely continues with further details.

?

Commit today to identify the type of proof (syllogism, induction, or analogy) behind one core belief you hold—and ask yourself if the suitability condition is met.
?
You have been given three tools for assent: syllogism, induction, and analogy. Which one do you rely on most to affirm your faith? Is that reliance a sign of intellectual depth or spiritual laziness? Before you finish this lesson, construct one short syllogism for a truth you believe and see if it passes the condition of suitability.

Classification of Rational and Transmitted Evidence
Al-Taftazani classifies evidence based on judgment type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Taftazani's classification of rational and transmitted evidence and the three cases determining which type is appropriate based on the nature of the desired judgment.

Core Idea
How to choose between reason and transmission depends on the intellect's stance on the judgment.

Al-Taftazani provides a clear rule for when to use rational vs transmitted evidence. The choice depends on whether the intellect naturally favors one side or remains balanced. This passage lays out the three possible scenarios and the proof method for each.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: definition first, then three cases, then a concluding remark.
The dash after 'الصانع' is a punctuation break; treat it as a pause.
2
Understand the two categories of evidence
Identify the two categories: rational evidence (implication by reason's judgment) and transmitted evidence (otherwise). This is the starting point for the classification.
3
Examine the three cases for the desired judgment
Walk through each case: (1) intellect balanced → transmission only, (2) transmission depends on the judgment → reason only, (3) otherwise → both. Note the examples given for each.
Pay attention to the logical reason for case 2: avoiding circularity.
4
Reflect on the concluding statement
Read the final sentence about when reason and transmission support each other. Consider what 'yields knowledge or not' means in terms of certainty.
This hints at the possibility of probabilistic proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطريق القسمة أن استلزامه للمطلوب إن كان بحكم العقل فعقلي، وإلا فنقلي ثم الحكم المطلوب إن استوى فيه عند العقل جانب الثبوت والانتماء بحيث لا يجد من نفسه سبيلا إلى تعيين أحدهما فطريق إثباته النقل لا غير، كالحكم بوجوب الحج ، ويكون زيده في الدار، وإلا فإن توقف عليه ثبوت النقل كالعلم بصدق المخبر، وما يبتنى عليه ذلك، كثبوت الصانع - وبعثة النبي ودلالة المعجزة، ونحو ذلك فطريق إثباته العقل لا غير، لئلا يلزم الدور، وإلا فيمكن إثباته بكل من النقل والعقل كوحدة الصانع، وحدوث العالم، إذا صح الاستدلال على الصانع بإمكان العالم أو بحدوث الأعراض، أو بعض الجواهر، وإذا تعاضد العقل والنقل كان المثبت ما أفاد العلم أو لا
The method of division is that the implication of the evidence for the desired conclusion: if it is by reason's judgment, then it is rational; otherwise, it is transmitted. Then the desired judgment: if intellect is equal regarding it—the side of affirmation and negation—such that it does not find from itself a way to determine one of them, then the way of proving it is transmission alone, like the ruling of the obligation of pilgrimage and 'Zayd is in the house'. Otherwise, if the establishment of transmission depends on it, like knowledge of the truthfulness of the reporter and what is built upon that, such as the existence of the Maker, the sending of the Prophet, the indication of the miracle, and the like, then the way of proving it is reason alone, to avoid circularity. Otherwise, it can be proven by both transmission and reason, like the oneness of the Maker and the origination of the world, if the inference to the Maker by the possibility of the world or by the originatedness of accidents or some substances is valid. And when reason and transmission support each other, what is affirmed is what yields knowledge or not.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage provides a classification (qisma) of evidence based on the nature of the desired judgment. The author first defines the two categories: rational evidence is that whose implication for the conclusion is by reason's judgment; transmitted evidence is that which is not by reason's judgment. Then he explains the rule for using each. If the intellect is balanced, i.e., it does not incline more to affirming or negating the judgment (it finds no way to determine which side), then only transmission can establish it. Examples are the obligation of pilgrimage (a legal ruling not known by pure reason) and the contingent fact 'Zayd is in the house' (which cannot be known by reason alone). If transmission itself depends on something (i.e., the validity of transmission relies on that judgment), then that something must be proven by reason alone to avoid circularity. Examples are knowledge of the truthfulness of the reporter, which itself requires rational proof of the existence of God, prophethood, and miracles. Thus, these foundational truths are proven by reason only. For other judgments, like the oneness of God and the world's createdness, both reason and transmission can be used if valid proofs exist. Finally, when both reason and transmission concur, the resulting proof is what yields knowledge or not (i.e., it may yield certainty or probability, depending on the case).

Key Terms
العقل
النقل
الدليل العقلي
الدليل النقلي
الحكم المطلوب
استواء العقل
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational and transmitted evidence are always interchangeable and can be used for any judgment.
Correction: The author shows that some judgments require one type exclusively depending on the intellect's stance or the risk of circularity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when must the proof be rational only, not transmitted?
A) When the judgment is about obvious matters.
B) When transmission itself depends on that judgment (to avoid circularity).
C) When the judgment is about legal rulings.
D) When intellect is balanced.

Key Takeaway
Choosing between rational and transmitted evidence depends on whether the intellect is balanced, whether transmission relies on the judgment, or neither.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The passage says that the existence of the Maker must be proven by reason alone—cannot rely on transmission. Have you ever thought about the foundation of your own belief in God? Is it built on rational conviction or on inherited acceptance? What would change if you examined it rationally?
?
Al-Taftazani warns against circular reasoning in proving the truthfulness of the Prophet. Can you think of a time when you used a circular argument in your faith? How can you ensure that your belief in the Prophet is grounded in sound reasoning, not just repetition?
