Rational Opposition Blocks Certainty from Transmitted Evidence
Al-Taftazani enumerates and refutes possible responses to rational contradiction, concluding that certainty from transmitted evidence is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possibility of rational contradiction prevents transmitted evidence from producing certainty in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Certainty requires ruling out rational opposition, which is impossible.

This lesson tackles a foundational problem in theological epistemology: how can we be certain of transmitted reports when reason might contradict them? Al-Taftazani argues that certainty is impossible because we can never be sure that no rational opposition exists. The passage systematically eliminates the possible responses to a rational contradiction, showing that each leads to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without translating. Notice the structure: the author states a condition, then lists three impossible options, then explains why the third is impossible, and concludes.
The last sentence is incomplete but the argument is clear.
2
Identify the three impossible options
The passage presents three ways one might try to handle a rational contradiction: affirming both transmitted evidence and reason, denying both, or affirming one and denying the other. Find these in the text.
3
Understand why affirming transmitted and denying reason is impossible
The author argues that reason is the foundation of transmitted evidence. Denying reason would undermine the basis for trusting transmission. Use the commentary to grasp this key point.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that transmitted evidence cannot produce knowledge (ilm) because affirming it would require denying reason. Ask yourself: what does this imply for theological certainty?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن جملة ما لا بد منه ولا سبيل إلى الجزم به انتفاء المعارض العقلي، إذ مع وجوده يجب تأويل النقل وصرفه عن ظاهره لأنه لا يجوز تصديقهما لامتناع اعتقاد حقية النقيضين، ولا تكذيبهما لامتناع اعتقاد بطلان النقيضين ولا تصديق النقل وتكذيب العقل، لأنه أصل النقل لاحتياجه إليه وانتهائه بالآخرة إليه لما سبق من معرفة صدق النقل والفرع جميعاً، وما يفضي وجوده إلى عدمه باطل قطعاً، واقتصر في المتن على هذا لكونه وافياً بتمام المقصود. وذلك لأنه لما امتنع تصديق النقل لاستلزامه تكذيب العقل الذي هو الأصل ثبت أنه لا يفيد العلم، أنه لا بد من
Among what is necessary and for which there is no way to be certain is the absence of rational opposition, for with its existence there must be interpretation of the transmitted evidence and turning it from its apparent meaning, because it is not permissible to affirm both due to the impossibility of believing the truth of two contradictories, nor to deny both due to the impossibility of believing the falsehood of two contradictories, nor to affirm the transmitted and deny reason, because it is the origin of transmission due to its need for it and its ultimate dependence on it as preceded from knowing the truthfulness of both transmission and branch, and whatever leads to the existence of its own non-existence is absolutely invalid. The text confined itself to this because it suffices for the complete purpose. That is because when affirmation of transmitted evidence is impossible due to its necessitating denial of reason, which is the origin, it is established that it does not produce knowledge, and it is necessary that...
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage addresses the problem of rational contradiction (mu'arid 'aqli) as a barrier to certainty from transmitted evidence (naql). It states that among the necessary conditions, and one that is impossible to be certain about, is the absence of rational opposition. When such opposition exists, naql must be interpreted (ta'wil) and turned from its apparent meaning (zahir). The author gives a logical argument: one cannot affirm both (reason and naql) because that would require believing two contradictories are both true, which is impossible. Similarly, one cannot deny both, as that would require believing both are false. One also cannot affirm naql and deny reason because reason is the foundation (asl) of naql—naql depends on reason and ultimately returns to it, since knowledge of naql's truthfulness relies on reason. Any position that leads to the non-existence of reason is absolutely invalid. The author notes that the text (matn) confined itself to this argument because it suffices to show that naql does not yield certainty. The conclusion is that since affirming naql necessitates denying reason, naql cannot produce real knowledge (ilm). The final sentence is cut off but the argument is clear: certainty is impossible.

Key Terms

تأويل (ta'wil)
ظاهر (zahir)
نقيض (naqid)
أصل (asl)
فرع (far')
تصديق (tasdiq)

باطل (batil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that when reason and transmitted evidence conflict, one can simply accept both as true in different senses.
Correction: The passage explains that two contradictories cannot both be true, so simultaneous affirmation is impossible. One must be rejected or reinterpreted.

Checkpoint
Why can we not accept both rational opposition and transmitted evidence as true when they contradict?
A) Because reason is weaker than transmitted evidence
B) Because it is impossible to believe two contradictories are both true
C) Because transmitted evidence can always be reinterpreted
D) Because reason is not the foundation of transmitted evidence

Key Takeaway
Because rational opposition can never be ruled out, transmitted evidence alone cannot produce certainty; it must yield to reason through interpretation.

Argument That Transmitted Evidence Cannot Yield Certainty
Proves that transmitted evidence cannot yield certainty due to possible rational contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that transmitted evidence cannot yield certainty because rational contradiction is always possible and reason is the foundation of transmission.

Core Idea
Transmitted evidence depends on reason, so it cannot be certain.

Sanusi presents a powerful objection: if transmitted evidence could ever contradict reason, we would face an impossible choice. This lesson walks through the reasoning that leads to the conclusion that transmitted evidence can only yield conjecture, not certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and the English translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
2
Identify the key claim
Notice the opening statement that certainty about conditions and absence of impediments is impossible. This sets up the argument.
3
Trace the logical steps
Follow the reasoning: if rational contradiction exists, we must interpret the transmitted evidence figuratively. The text explains three impossible scenarios. Understand each one.
4
Understand the conclusion
See how the author concludes that transmitted evidence does not yield knowledge because believing it would require disbelieving reason, which is its foundation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة فلا سبيل إلى الجزم بوجود الشرائط وعدم الموانع، بل غايته الظن، وما يبتنى على الظن لا يفيد إلا الظن ومن جملة ما لا بد منه ولا سبيل إلى الجزم به انتفاء المعارض العقلي، إذ مع وجوده يجب تأويل النقل وصرفه عن ظاهره لأنه لا يجوز تصديقهما لامتناع اعتقاد حقية النقيضين، ولا تكذيبهما لامتناع اعتقاد بطلان النقيضين ولا تصديق النقل وتكذيب العقل، لأنه أصل النقل لاحتياجه إليه وانتهائه بالآخرة إليه لما سبق من معرفة صدق النقل والفرع جميعاً، وما يفضي وجوده إلى عدمه باطل قطعاً، واقتصر في المتن على هذا لكونه وافياً بتمام المقصود. وذلك لأنه لما امتنع تصديق النقل لاستلزامه تكذيب العقل الذي هو الأصل ثبت أنه لا يفيد العلم.
In summary: there is no way to be certain of the existence of the conditions and the absence of impediments; rather, its ultimate [outcome] is conjecture, and what is based on conjecture yields only conjecture. Among what is necessary and for which there is no way to be certain is the absence of rational contradiction. For if it exists, it is necessary to interpret transmitted evidence figuratively and turn it from its apparent meaning, because it is not permissible to believe both [reason and revelation], since believing the truthfulness of two contradictory statements is impossible, nor to disbelieve both, since believing the falsity of two contradictories is impossible, nor to believe transmitted evidence and disbelieve reason, because reason is the foundation of transmitted evidence, due to its need for reason and its ultimate recourse to it, as has preceded concerning the knowledge of the truthfulness of the transmitter and the transmitted together. And whatever [thing] whose existence leads to its own non-existence is absolutely false. And the [author] restricted [the discussion] in the text to this because it suffices for the entire purpose. That is because when it became impossible to believe transmitted evidence due to its necessitating disbelieving reason, which is the foundation, it is established that [transmitted evidence] does not yield knowledge.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that transmitted evidence (al-dalīl al-naqlī) cannot yield certainty (ʿilm). The argument follows a step-by-step logical chain: (1) We cannot be certain that all conditions for the validity of transmitted evidence exist or that all impediments are absent; we only reach conjecture (ẓann). (2) One necessary condition is the absence of any rational contradiction (al-muʿāriḍ al-ʿaqlī). (3) If a rational contradiction arose, we would have to interpret the transmitted evidence figuratively, because it is impossible to believe both reason and revelation if they contradict (since that would require believing two contradictories), impossible to disbelieve both (since that would require believing both false), and impossible to believe the transmitted and disbelieve reason, because reason is the foundation (aṣl) of transmission—transmitted evidence depends on reason for its validation. (4) Therefore, anything that leads to its own non-existence (i.e., accepting the transmitted at the expense of reason would undermine the very foundation of the transmitted) is false. (5) Hence, transmitted evidence cannot yield knowledge. The author notes that he has restricted the argument to this concise form because it is sufficient.

Key Terms
ظن
معارض عقلي
نقيض
أصل النقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is an absolute denial that transmitted evidence can ever be certain in any context.
Correction: This is a preliminary argument that will be refuted later; the author is presenting an objection, not a final position.

Checkpoint
According to this argument, why is it impossible to believe both transmitted evidence (naql) and rational evidence (ʿaql) when they contradict?
A) Because reason is always superior to revelation.
B) Because believing two contradictories is impossible.
C) Because transmitted evidence is inherently unreliable.
D) Because conjecture cannot lead to certainty.

Key Takeaway
Transmitted evidence cannot yield certainty because it is ultimately dependent on reason, and any rational contradiction forces a figurative interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a Quranic verse or hadith that seems to contradict what your mind finds rational, do you immediately dismiss your intellect or do you patiently seek a reconciliation? How does this lesson urge you to hold both in tension?
?
The author says reason is the foundation of transmitted evidence. Have you ever treated revelation as self-sufficient without engaging your reason? In what area of your faith might you be ignoring the role of sound reasoning, and what would it look like to rectify that starting today?
?
This lesson argues that without the condition of no rational contradiction, transmitted evidence yields only conjecture. How often do you accept religious claims without examining whether they conflict with clear rational principles? What specific step will you take this week to think more critically about a belief you hold?

The Fourth Option: Suspension of Judgment
Refutes the previous proof by allowing suspension of both evidences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that when reason and transmitted evidence appear to conflict, it is not necessary to believe or disbelieve either; one can suspend judgment by treating both as invalid, and that this refutes the previous argument.

Core Idea
Suspension of judgment is a valid rational option when proofs conflict.

In the previous lesson, an argument was presented that attempted to undermine the certainty of transmitted evidence by requiring that when reason and transmitted evidence conflict, one must either believe both, disbelieve both, or believe one and disbelieve the other—all leading to problematic conclusions. Now, the author refutes this by pointing out that a fourth option exists: we can judge both proofs as invalid and suspend judgment. This lesson explores that logical opening and clarifies what takdhīb (disbelief) truly means.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Get a sense of the refutation's flow.
Notice the key terms: تساقط, توقف, تكذيب.
2
Identify the assumed three options and the fourth
The passage first states what the previous argument claimed: that we must either believe both, disbelieve both, or believe one and disbelieve the other. Then it says 'لجواز أن يحكم بتساقطهما'. Find this fourth option and understand it.
The fourth option is to judge both proofs as invalid and treat them as non-existent.
3
Understand the distinction between takdhīb and ʿadam al-taṣdīq
The passage clarifies that even if takdhīb is equated with non-belief, disbelieving both does not require believing the two contradictories are eliminated. This shows that suspension is the only necessary consequence.
Takdhīb is active disbelief; ʿadam al-taṣdīq is simply not accepting the proof.
4
Recognize the redundancy argument
The passage concludes that disbelieving both actually entails the desired conclusion (that transmitted evidence does not yield knowledge), so the previous argument's conclusion is redundant.
This strengthens the refutation: the argument does not prove its point uniquely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وباقي المقدمات مع ما في الحصر من المناقشة إذ لا يلزم تصديقهما أو تكذيبهما، أو تصديق أحدهما وتكذيب الآخر، لجواز أن يحكم بتساقطهما، وكونهما في حكم العدم، من غير يعتقد معهما حقية شيء أو بطلانه، ولو جعل التكذيب مساوياً لعدم التصديق لم يلزم من تكذيب العقل والنقل اعتقاد ارتفاع النقيضين وبطلانهما، لأن معنى عدم تصديق الدليل عدم اعتقاد صحته واستلزامه لحقية النتيجة، وهذا لا يستلزم بطلانها أو اعتقاد بطلانها وارتفاعها فغاية الأمر التوقف في الإثبات والنفي، على أن تكذيبهما أيضاً يستلزم المطلوب، أعني عدم إفادة النقل العلم فنفيه يكون مستدركاً في البيان
And the remaining premises, along with what is in the restrictive enumeration, are open to objection: because it is not necessary to believe both or disbelieve both, or believe one and disbelieve the other, for it is possible to judge that both are invalid and that they are in the status of non-existence, without thereby believing the truth or falsity of anything. And if disbelief (al-takdhīb) is made equivalent to non-belief (ʿadam al-taṣdīq), then disbelieving reason and transmitted evidence does not necessitate believing the elimination of the two contradictories and their falsity, because the meaning of not believing a proof is not believing its correctness and its entailment of the truth of the result, and this does not necessitate its falsity or believing its falsity and elimination. So the utmost [that follows] is suspension in affirmation and negation. Moreover, disbelieving both also entails the desired conclusion, namely that transmitted evidence does not yield knowledge, so its negation becomes redundant in the explanation.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage refutes the previous argument by pointing out a logical oversight. The previous argument assumed only three options when reason and transmitted evidence appear to contradict: believe both, disbelieve both, or believe one and disbelieve the other. The refutation shows that a fourth option exists: we can judge both proofs to be invalid (tasāquṭ) and treat them as null, without committing to the truth or falsity of either. This is a suspension of judgment (tawaqquf). Furthermore, even if we define 'disbelief' as simply 'non-belief' (i.e., not accepting the proof rather than actively declaring it false), then disbelieving both does not require believing in the elimination of the two contradictories. The consequence is that the earlier argument's conclusion ('transmitted evidence does not yield knowledge') is actually already implied even by disbelieving both, so it is redundant (mustadrak) – that is, the argument does not prove its point uniquely.

Key Terms
تساقط
توقف
تكذيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: When reason and transmitted evidence conflict, one must either accept both literally, accept one and reject the other, or reject both—with no other option.
Correction: The refutation shows that one can suspend judgment by treating both as invalid without committing to truth or falsity.

Checkpoint
What fourth option does this refutation introduce that the previous argument overlooked?
A) Believing both reason and transmitted evidence literally.
B) Reinterpreting the transmitted evidence figuratively.
C) Judging both proofs to be invalid and suspending judgment.
D) Accepting transmitted evidence and rejecting reason.

Key Takeaway
When reason and transmitted evidence conflict, one can suspend judgment by treating both as invalid, and this is distinct from disbelieving them; thus the previous argument's claim is refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an apparent conflict between reason and a revealed truth in your own life, do you feel pressured to quickly choose a side (believe one, disbelieve both)? Have you ever considered the possibility of humble suspension—admitting you don't know—without feeling defeated? Will you commit to practicing tawaqquf next time you encounter a difficult theological question?
?
The text says the utmost is 'suspension in affirmation and negation.' Are you comfortable living with unanswered questions about your faith? Where in your heart do you feel a need to force certainty where God has not given it? Pray for the patience to wait for clarity rather than rushing to a false conclusion.
?
This passage refutes a forced dichotomy. In your spiritual life, are there areas where you feel pressured to choose between two extremes (e.g., rationalism vs. fideism, strict literalism vs. complete rejection)? Will you embrace the third way of tawaqquf, trusting that the truth is with Allah and we may not need to decide immediately?

The Self-Evident Concept of Existence and Verbal Definitions
Al-Taftazani affirms the self-evidence of existence and discusses verbal definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of existence is self-evident and why any definition of it can only be verbal, not real.

Core Idea
Existence is so basic it cannot be defined — only pointed to by synonyms.

Al-Taftazani opens the chapter on existence by declaring that its conception is self-evident. This means every rational person already knows what 'existence' means without needing a definition. But if it's self-evident, why do scholars give definitions? In this passage, he explains that such definitions are merely verbal aids for understanding the word, not the concept itself.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the claim of self-evidence
Read the opening lines where al-Taftazani asserts that the conception of existence is self-evident by necessity. Note that he says even the judgment (that existence exists) is also self-evident.
The author contrasts this with those who think it is acquired (kash).
2
Understand the philosophers' inductive argument
The majority of philosophers relied on induction: the intellect finds nothing more known than existence, so it is the clearest thing. Grasp this argument.
3
Distinguish real vs. verbal definition
Al-Taftazani explains that a real definition (hadīqī) would be circular because existence is already known. Instead, we use verbal definitions with synonyms like kawn, thubut, etc., for someone who knows these words but not 'wujud'.
The passage ends discussing definitions like 'al-thabit al-'ayn' and questions if they are meant as real (rasmi).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The first chapter on existence and non-existence. (He said): The first chapter on existence and non-existence, containing investigations: the first investigation: the conception of existence is self-evident by necessity. And defining it with such as kawn (being), thubut (establishedness), tahqiq (realness), shay'iyya (thingness), and with such as al-thabit al-'ayn (the fixed entity), and what can be reported about and known, or is divided into agent and patient, or ancient and originated, is a definition by what is more obscure while it is true of the existent. Among them are those who claim that the judgment is acquired. He arranged the second intended topic into three chapters on existence, essence, and their consequences. The first chapter includes the investigation of non-existence, and the truth is that the conception of existence is self-evident, and that this judgment is also self-evident, every rational person who turns his attention to it is certain of it, even if he has not practiced methods of acquisition, to the point that the majority of the philosophers held that nothing is more known than existence, and they relied on induction, for it is sufficient for this goal, because if the intellect does not find among its intellected things what is more known than it—rather it is in its rank—it is established that it is the clearest of things according to the intellect. And the clear meaning may be known in terms of being a denotation of a word without [being the denotation of] another word, so it is defined by a verbal definition that conveys its understanding from that word, not its conception in itself, so that there would be circularity and definition of the thing by itself. That is like their definition of existence with kawn, thubut, tahqiq, shay'iyya, and husul (occurrence) and the like, with respect to one who knows the meaning of existence in terms of these words being its denotation rather than the word 'wujud', so that if it were reversed it would be reversed. As for the definition with 'al-thabit al-'ayn' or with 'that which can be reported about and known' or with 'that which is divided into agent and patient' or with 'that which is divided into ancient and originated', if it is intended to be a real definition (rasmi), then it requires
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument. It begins with the heading 'الفصل الأول في الوجود والعدم' and the author's statement that the conception of existence is self-evident. The commentator then explains that the truth is that both the conception and the judgment of existence are self-evident. He supports this by noting that the majority of philosophers hold that nothing is more known than existence, relying on induction because the intellect finds nothing more known. Then he clarifies that clear concepts like existence can only be defined verbally to avoid circularity—for example, by using synonyms like kawn, thubut, etc., for someone who knows existence via those words. He then discusses definitions that use phrases like 'al-thabit al-'ayn' or divisions into agent/patient or ancient/originated, and says that if intended as a real definition (rasmi), a problem arises (the text cuts off). The key point is that existence is the most basic concept and cannot be genuinely defined.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage shows existence is self-evident; any attempt at a real definition would be circular. Definitions given are merely verbal aids for those unfamiliar with the word 'wujud'.

Why does the commentator argue that a real definition (رسمي) of existence is problematic?

D) Because definitions are unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
Existence is the most fundamental concept; it is self-evident and can only be defined verbally using synonyms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim that existence is self-evident, but do you live with the same certainty that God—the Necessary Existent—is the foundation of your existence? Is your awareness of His existence as immediate and undeniable as your awareness of your own existence? If not, what is blocking that certainty? Commit to reciting 'Allah, there is no god but He' with full presence until it becomes self-evident to your heart.
?
The philosophers held that nothing is more known than existence, yet how often do you doubt your own worth or purpose, forgetting that your very existence is a gift from the One who is Most Known? In what area of your life are you looking for a definition of your identity when the answer is simply to be present and grateful for the fact that you are? Resolve to stop seeking validation and instead rest in the self-evident truth that you exist only for Him.
?
This passage warns against defining existence by its divisions, like ancient and originated. Yet you perhaps define God by what He does for you (sustenance, forgiveness) rather than by His very existence as the only true reality. Are you treating God as a means to your ends, forgetting that He is the End? Today, spend five minutes contemplating the phrase 'لا إله إلا الله' not as a statement of belief but as the self-evident truth that nothing else exists in the way He does. Let it transform how you see everything else.

The Self-Evidence of Existence and the Futility of Defining It
Al-Taftazani argues for the self-evidence of existence and critiques definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani holds that the conception of existence is self-evident and why attempts to define it are either circular or not clearer.

Core Idea
Existence is the most known concept; any definition is either circular or fails to be clearer.

Al-Taftazani begins with a bold claim: the very idea of existence is self-evident, accepted by every rational person. He then spends the rest of the passage dismantling various attempts to define existence, showing they either presuppose what they are trying to define or are more obscure. This lesson walks you through his argument and his critique of common definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening assertion
Start by reading the first few lines where al-Taftazani states that both the conception of existence and the judgment that it is self-evident are intuitive.
Notice that he says every rational person agrees, even without training.
2
Understand the philosophical consensus
The passage mentions that most philosophers rely on induction (al-istiqrāʾ) to conclude that nothing is more known than existence.
3
Identify the two types of definitions
Al-Taftazani distinguishes between verbal definitions (taʿrīf lafẓī) and real definitions (taʿrīf rasmī). Observe how he treats each.
Verbal definitions are acceptable; real definitions are problematic.
4
Follow the critique of real definitions
The passage details why definitions like 'the fixed entity' lead to circularity or are not clearer. Pay attention to the objection and its rebuttal.
Al-Taftazani considers a counterargument and dismisses it as weak.

خبير بأنه لا دلالة للفظ عينه على هذا المعنى، ولا يعقل من الثابت إلا ماله الثبوت، وهو معنى الموجود، وكون هذه التعريفات للوجود هو ظاهر كلام التجريد والمباحث المشرقية وفي كلام المتقدمين أن الموجود هو الثابت العين والمعدوم هو المنفي العين، وكأن زيادة لفظ العين لدفع توهم أن يراد الثابت لشيء والمنفي عن شيء فإن ذلك معنى المحمول لا
The truth is that the conception of existence is self-evident, and that this judgment [that it is self-evident] is also self-evident, assented to by every rational person who attends to it, even if he has not practiced the methods of acquisition, such that the majority of philosophers held that nothing is more known than existence, and they relied on induction, for it suffices for this purpose, because if the intellect does not find in its concepts what is more known than it—rather it is in its rank—it is established that it is the clearest of things to the intellect. And the clear meaning may be known insofar as it is the signification of a word without [another] word, so it is known by a verbal definition that conveys an understanding of it from that word, not its conception in itself, which would be circular, and a definition of the thing by itself. That is like their definition of existence by 'occurrence' (al-kawn), 'fixity' (al-thubūt), 'reality' (al-taḥqīq), 'thingness' (al-shayʾiyya), 'obtaining' (al-ḥuṣūl), and the like, relative to one who knows the meaning of existence insofar as it is the signification of these words without the word 'existence', such that if it were reversed it would be reversed. As for the definition by 'the fixed entity' (al-thābit al-ʿayn), or by 'that which can be reported of and known', or by 'that which divides into agent and patient', or by 'that which divides into the eternal and the temporal'—if it is intended as a real definition, then the necessity of circularity is apparent, for the meaning of 'that which is fixed' and 'that which can' and the like cannot be conceived except after conceiving the meaning of 'obtaining' in external entities or minds. And even if granted, there is no doubt that the meaning of existence is clearer to the intellect than the meanings of these expressions. And the circularity may be established by [saying] that the described subject supposed for these attributes—namely, 'that which is fixed', 'that which can', 'that which divides'—is existence itself, because its other is either the existent (al-mawjūd) or non-existence (al-ʿadam) or the non-existent (al-maʿdūm), and none of these applies to existence—but this is weak, because concepts are not limited to what was mentioned, so it is possible to suppose like 'meaning' (al-maʿnā), 'matter' (al-amr), and 'thing' (al-shayʾ) which apply to existence and other than it. And it is weak because concepts are not limited to what was mentioned, so it is possible to suppose like 'meaning', 'matter', and 'thing' which apply to existence and other than it. The intent of [؟]. And if it is intended as a nominal definition, then there is no doubt that it is not clearer in indicating the word 'existence', but rather more obscure, so it is not suitable as a nominal definition, just as it is not suitable as a real one, despite that each of them applies to the existent, and some of them to the very entities of existents. And one may strain to deny that 'the fixed entity' applies to existents, by [saying] that its meaning is 'the fixed in its essence' (al-thābit ʿaynahu), i.e., its self (nafsuhu) as it is, not by virtue of another matter, unlike the existent, for it is fixed by virtue of its being qualified by existence. Thus 'the fixed' is more general, whether it is fixed by itself, which is existence, or by existence, which is existents. And you are aware that the word 'itself' (ʿaynahu) has no indication of this meaning, and nothing is understood from 'the fixed' except that which has fixity, which is the meaning of the existent. And that these definitions are for existence is apparent from the words of the Tajrīd and the Eastern Investigations, and in the words of the earlier scholars that the existent is the fixed entity and the non-existent is the negated entity, and as if the addition of the word 'entity' (al-ʿayn) is to repel the misunderstanding that what is intended is 'fixed for something' and 'negated of something', for that is the meaning of the predicate, not
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument by al-Taftazani that the concept of existence (al-wujūd) is self-evident (badīhī) and a critique of various attempts to define it. The passage begins by asserting that both the conception of existence and the judgment that it is self-evident are intuitive, accepted by every rational person, and that the majority of philosophers held that nothing is more known (aʿraf) than existence. They relied on induction (al-istiqrāʾ) because if the intellect does not find something more known in its concepts, then existence is the clearest. Al-Taftazani then explains that a clear meaning can be known through a verbal definition (taʿrīf lafẓī) that points to the same meaning using different words, to avoid circularity (dawr). This is like defining existence with synonyms such as 'occurrence' (al-kawn), 'fixity' (al-thubūt), etc., for someone who knows existence through those words. Such definitions are merely verbal. However, real definitions (taʿrīf rasmī) like 'the fixed entity' (al-thābit al-ʿayn), 'that which can be reported of and known', etc., lead to circularity because understanding them requires prior knowledge of existence. Even if that were granted, existence is still clearer. Al-Taftazani then presents an objection: the circularity argument might be countered by noting that the subject of those descriptions is not necessarily existence; other concepts like 'meaning' (maʿnā), 'matter' (amr), and 'thing' (shayʾ) could serve as the subject. He dismisses this objection as weak (ḍaʿīf) because those concepts also apply to existence and other things, so the circularity remains. He then considers if these definitions are intended as nominal (ismī) definitions—but they are not clearer than the word 'existence', so they fail. He also discusses a strained argument that 'fixed entity' does not apply to existents because 'fixed entity' means fixed in itself, while existents are fixed by existence. Al-Taftazani refutes this by noting that the word 'itself' (ʿaynahu) does not convey that meaning, and 'fixed' simply means having fixity, which is the meaning of 'existent'. He concludes that these definitions are indeed for existence, as seen in the Tajrīd and al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya, and that the addition of 'entity' (al-ʿayn) is to prevent misunderstanding that 'fixed' might mean 'fixed for something' (a predicate), not the subject itself.

Key Terms
الوجود (al-wujūd)
التصور (al-taṣawwur)
بديهي (badīhī)
تعريف لفظي (taʿrīf lafẓī)
تعريف رسمي (taʿrīf rasmī)
دور (dawr)
ثابت العين (thābit al-ʿayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that existence can be clearly defined by phrases like 'the fixed entity' without circularity.
Correction: The passage shows that such definitions either presuppose the concept of existence or are not clearer than 'existence' itself, making them unsuitable as definitions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main problem with defining existence as 'the fixed entity' (al-thābit al-ʿayn) if it is intended as a real definition?
A) It is not clear enough and leads to circularity.
B) It only applies to non-existents.
C) It is a verbal definition, not a real one.
D) It contradicts the views of earlier scholars.

Key Takeaway
Existence is self-evident and cannot be defined without circularity or obscurity; any attempt to define it either presupposes it or fails to be clearer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence is the most known concept, why do you often seek intellectual proofs for your own existence or God's existence instead of resting in its self-evidence? What would change if you allowed yourself to simply affirm existence without defining it?
?
Your mind loves to define and categorize. But this lesson shows that existence defies definition. Where in your life are you more concerned with defining God or your faith rather than living in the direct awareness of His existence? How can you practice the humility of not needing to define everything?

First Argument for Self-Evidence of Existence and Its Refutation
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes the first argument for the self-evidence of the concept of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument for the self-evidence of the conception of existence and how al-Taftazani refutes it by distinguishing between the self-evidence of a judgment and the self-evidence of its component concepts.

Core Idea
Self-evidence of a judgment does not guarantee self-evidence of its concepts.

Al-Taftazani begins examining claims that the conception of existence is self-evident. The first argument appeals to the self-evident judgment that existence and non-existence are incompatible. He refutes this by clarifying what 'self-evidence' means and showing that the argument either begs the question or fails to prove its point. In this lesson, we follow his precise logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the full English translation. Try to grasp the overall flow of argument and response before diving into details.
The passage is dense; do not worry if it is not immediately clear.
2
Identify the argument's structure
The argument claims: The self-evident judgment of the incompatibility of existence and non-existence depends on conceiving existence; therefore, the conception of existence is self-evident. Identify the premise and conclusion.
Note that the argument tries to infer the self-evidence of a concept from the self-evidence of a judgment.
3
Analyze the refutation
Al-Taftazani refutes by distinguishing absolute self-evidence (no acquisition) from self-evidence of a judgment (the judgment itself is immediate). He shows the argument either assumes what it needs to prove or does not entail the desired conclusion.
Focus on the distinction: the self-evidence of the judgment does not require the concepts to be self-evident.
4
Understand the objection and response
An objector suggests that the self-evidence of the whole (the judgment) includes the self-evidence of its parts (the concepts). Al-Taftazani replies that knowledge of the whole's self-evidence does not require separate knowledge of each part's self-evidence; the part's self-evidence is constitutive but not necessarily known separately.
This refines the distinction between the self-evidence of a composite and its components.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : واستدل بوجوه الأول : أن التصديق البديهي بتنافي الوجود والعدم يتوقف على تصوره، ورد بأنه أريد البداهة مطلقاً بمعنى عدم التوقف على الكسب أصلاً فممنوع، بل مصادرة أو بداهة الحكم فغير مفيد إذ لا يستلزم تصور الحقيقة ولا ينافي اكتسابه لا يقال بداهة الكل وان توقفت على بداهة الأجزاء لكن العلم ببداهته لا يتوقف على العلم ببداهتها بل يستتبعه فلا مضادرة) لأنا نقول توقف العلم ببداهة الكل على العلم ببداهة الجزء ضروري كتوقف الكل على الجزء إذ بداهة الجزء جزء بداهة الكل، والعلم بالكل إما نفس العلم بالأجزاء أو حاصل به) .
He said: And he argued with aspects: The first: that the self-evident affirmation of the incompatibility of existence and non-existence depends on their conception. And it was refuted by [the reply] that if absolute self-evidence is intended—meaning no dependence on acquisition at all—then it is denied; rather it is a petition of principle. Or if the self-evidence of the judgment [is intended], it is not useful because it does not entail the conception of the reality nor contradict its being acquired. It cannot be said: the self-evidence of the whole, even if it depends on the self-evidence of the parts, but knowledge of its self-evidence does not depend on knowledge of their self-evidence; rather it is entailed by it, so there is no petition of principle. For we say: the dependence of knowledge of the self-evidence of the whole on knowledge of the self-evidence of the part is necessary, just as the whole depends on the part, since the self-evidence of the part is part of the self-evidence of the whole. And knowledge of the whole is either identical to knowledge of the parts or obtained through them.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the claim that the conception of existence is self-evident (badihiyy). The argument is that the self-evident judgment that existence and non-existence are incompatible presupposes the conception of existence; therefore the conception is even more self-evident. Al-Taftazani refutes this by distinguishing between two meanings of 'self-evidence'. If absolute self-evidence is meant—i.e., the judgment is known without any prior acquisition—then the argument is either denied or circular. If the self-evidence of the judgment itself is meant, that does not entail that the concepts involved are self-evident; they could be acquired. The objection that the self-evidence of the whole implies the self-evidence of the parts is rejected because knowledge of the self-evidence of the whole does not require knowledge of the self-evidence of each part; rather the self-evidence of the parts is constitutive but not necessarily known separately. Thus the argument fails.

Key Terms
بداهة
تصور الوجود
التصديق البديهي
مصادرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The self-evidence of a composite judgment necessarily entails the self-evidence of its component concepts.
Correction: The self-evidence of a judgment does not require the self-evidence of its constituent concepts; they may be acquired through prior reasoning.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw in the first argument for the self-evidence of the concept of existence?
A) It assumes that the judgment is self-evident without proof.
B) It confuses the self-evidence of the judgment with the self-evidence of the concepts.
C) It relies on a definition that is not acceptable.
D) It commits a logical fallacy of affirming the consequent.

Key Takeaway
The self-evidence of a judgment does not require the self-evidence of its constituent concepts; they may be acquired.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the concept of existence is not self-evident as this argument shows, then what assumptions about God's existence have you been building on a potentially shaky foundation? Are you willing to examine those assumptions today?
?
You probably treat many of your beliefs as self-evident—like that truth exists or that you are real. But this lesson forces you to ask: Have you ever honestly questioned whether those concepts are truly self-evident or merely comfortable habits of thought? Commit to identifying one belief you've never examined and investigate it.
?
This argument shows that even the most basic ideas can be misunderstood. What if your certainty about faith is built on a concept you haven't critically examined? Will you take the time to dig deeper, even if it feels uncomfortable?

The Third Argument: Existence as a Self-Evident Part and Its Refutation
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes the third argument that existence is self-evident as a part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that existence is a self-evident existential part is rejected by theologians.

Core Idea
Being a part does not make a concept self-evident.

Some have argued that since existence is a part of every existing thing, it must be self-evident. Al-Taftazani quickly dismantles this claim by distinguishing between conception and judgment, showing that neither yields a self-evident premise. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and refutation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the concise structure: one line for the argument, one for the refutation.
2
Understand the two interpretations
The refutation offers two possible interpretations of 'self-evident': conception or judgment. Consider why each fails.
Conception is not self-evident; judgment is not useful because it was already addressed.
3
Consider the stronger denial
Some even deny that existence can be conceived at all. Reflect on why this position exists.
This extreme view underscores the difficulty of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أنه جزء وجودي وهو بديهي، ورد بأنه إن أريد التصور فممنوع أو التصديق فغيره مفيد على ما سبق، وقيل لا يتصور أصلاً.
The third: that it is an existential part and it is self-evident. And it was refuted by [the reply] that if conception is intended, it is denied; or if affirmation, then it is not useful, as before. And it was said that it is not conceivable at all.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
The third argument claims that existence is a part of existent things and thus self-evident. The refutation is brief: if they mean the concept itself, it is not self-evident; if they mean the judgment, it has already been addressed. Some even deny that existence can be conceived at all.

Key Terms
جزء وجودي
بديهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That being a part of a self-evident whole makes a concept self-evident.
Correction: Being a part does not guarantee self-evidence; the concept itself must be immediately clear, which existence is not.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what is the problem with claiming existence is a self-evident part?
A) Existence is not a part of anything.
B) The conception of existence is not self-evident even if it is a part.
C) The judgment about existence is not self-evident.
D) Existence cannot be a part because it is simple.

Key Takeaway
The third argument fails because neither the conception nor the judgment of existence as a part is self-evident or useful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something like ‘existence’ is so basic, you already have a clear conception of it? When you say ‘I know God exists,’ are you sure it’s a clear conception or just an ingrained judgment? Which of these is truly self-evident to you, and which might need deeper examination? Will you take time today to question a belief you’ve treated as self-evident without fully understanding it, and trace it back to its conception?
?
The refutation distinguishes between the concept of existence and the judgment that it is self-evident. Where in your own faith or worldview might you be confusing the two? Is there a doctrinal statement you affirm passionately but cannot truly conceive? How can you move from mere affirmation to a clear conception without losing certainty?
?
This passage shows that even a simple argument about existence can fail if we don't carefully define our terms. What about your own claims—when you speak about God, the soul, or the afterlife, are you clear on whether you are referring to a concept or a judgment? Will you commit to examining the foundational beliefs of your faith this week, using the distinction between conception and affirmation to refine your understanding?

Al-Razi's Argument for Self-Evidence of Existence and Its Refutation
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes al-Razi's argument for the self-evidence of the concept of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's argument for the self-evidence of the concept of existence and al-Taftazani's refutation exposing it as a circular reasoning.

Core Idea
Self-evident judgments do not guarantee self-evident concepts.

Imam al-Razi attempted to prove that the concept of existence is self-evident by appealing to a self-evident judgment that uses that concept. Al-Taftazani carefully dissects this argument, revealing that it either commits a petition of principle or fails to establish the claim. This lesson walks through the argument and its refutation, sharpening your ability to detect hidden circularities.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Razi's argument
Read al-Razi's argument carefully. He claims that because the judgment 'existence and non-existence are incompatible' is self-evident and presupposes the concepts of existence and non-existence, those concepts are even more self-evident.
Notice that al-Razi starts from a judgment he considers self-evident.
2
Examine al-Taftazani's first interpretation and refutation
Al-Taftazani considers the case where the judgment includes its objects (the concepts) as per al-Razi's theory. In this case, the argument is circular (musadara) because the claim is used as a premise.
A petition of principle occurs when the conclusion is assumed in the premises.
3
Examine al-Taftazani's second interpretation and refutation
If the judgment alone is self-evident without including the concepts as parts, then it is conceded but does not prove that the concepts are self-evident. They could be acquired concepts merely conceived in some respect.
This shows that a self-evident judgment does not require self-evident concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان الإمام جعل التصديق ببداهة تصور الوجود كسبياً فاستدل عليه بوجوه . بديهي بجميع * : الأول : أن التصديق بأن الوجود والعدم متنافيان، لا يصدقان معاً على أمر أصلاً ، بل كل أمر فإما موجود أو معدوم تصديق بديهي، وهو مسبوق بتصور الوجود والعدم، فهو أولى بالبداهة والجواب أنه إن أريد أن هذا الحكم متعلقاته على ما هو رأي الإمام في التصديق فممنوع، بل مصادرة على المطلوب، حيث جعل المدعي وهو بداهة تصور الوجود جزءاً من الدليل، وإن أريد أن نفس الحكم بديهي بمعنى أنه لا يتوقف بعد تصور المتعلقات على كسب فمسلم لكنه لا يثبت المدعي وهو بداهة تصور الوجود بحقيقته لجواز الحكم أن يوجد (۳) الحكم البديهي مع عدم تصور الطرفين بالحقيقية بل بوجه ما ومع كون تصورهما كسبياً لا بديهياً، وإنما قلنا في الأول فممنوع بل مصادرة ولم تقتصر على أحدهما تنبيهاً على تمام الجواب بدون بيان المصادرة، وتحقيقاً للزوم المصادرة بأن (٤) بداهة كل جزء من أجزاء هذا
Imam [al-Razi] considered the affirmation of the self-evidence of the conception of existence to be acquired, so he argued for it with aspects. Self-evident in all aspects: the first: that the affirmation that existence and non-existence are incompatible—they are not both true of anything at all, rather every thing is either existent or non-existent—is a self-evident affirmation, and it is preceded by the conception of existence and non-existence, so it is more worthy of being self-evident. And the answer: that if it is intended that this judgment and its objects are as per Imam's view of affirmation, then it is denied, rather it is a petition of principle, where the claim—the self-evidence of the conception of existence—is made part of the proof. And if it is intended that the judgment itself is self-evident in the sense that it does not depend after the conception of the objects on acquisition, then it is conceded, but it does not prove the claim, which is the self-evidence of the conception of existence in its reality, because it is possible that the self-evident judgment exists without the conception of the two extremes in their reality, but only in some respect, and despite their conception being acquired not self-evident. And we said in the first [case] it is denied and a petition of principle, and we did not restrict ourselves to one of them to indicate the completeness of the answer without stating the petition, and to verify the necessity of the petition that the self-evidence of each part of this...
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
Imam al-Razi, who considered the concept of existence to be acquired, nonetheless gave an argument for its self-evidence: the judgment that existence and non-existence are incompatible is self-evident, and it presupposes the concepts of existence and non-existence, so those concepts are even more self-evident. Al-Taftazani refutes this by distinguishing two interpretations. If the judgment and its objects are taken as per al-Razi's theory of affirmation (which includes the concepts as parts), then the argument is circular because the claim is used as a premise. If the judgment alone is meant as self-evident, it is conceded but does not prove that the concepts are self-evident; they could be merely mentally conceived without being self-evident. Thus the argument fails.

Key Terms
إمام
بديهي بجميع
مصادرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a judgment is self-evident, then the concepts it contains must also be self-evident.
Correction: A self-evident judgment can involve concepts that are not self-evident; they may be merely conceived in some respect, and their full reality may be acquired.

Checkpoint
Why is al-Razi's argument considered a petition of principle (musadara)?
A) Because it assumes the concept of existence is self-evident without proof.
B) Because it uses the claim of self-evidence of existence as a premise in the proof.
C) Because it relies on a false premise about existence.
D) Because it contradicts the definition of existence.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's argument for the self-evidence of existence fails because it either assumes what it aims to prove or does not prove that the concepts are self-evident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a belief as 'self-evident' without fully examining the foundational concepts it depends on? What does this lesson reveal about your own intellectual habits? Will you now pause to question what you assume is obvious?
?
Al-Razi, a brilliant scholar, still fell into logical error. Where in your life do you most confidently assume you are correct? What would it cost you to admit that some of your core concepts might be less clear than you think?
?
The affirmation of God's existence is central to your faith. Is the concept of 'existence' itself truly clear to you? How does realizing that even a self-evident judgment can have partially conceived terms humble you to seek deeper understanding of what you profess?

The Third Point: Existence as Intuitive and the First Proof
Presents the third point, an objection, and the first proof for the intuitiveness of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third point about existence being intuitive and the first proof from the dichotomy of existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
The intuitiveness of existence is contested: is it conception or attestation?

This lesson introduces a key debate: Is the concept of existence intuitive, or must it be acquired? The author presents the third point and an objection, then shares Imam al-Razi's first proof from the self-evident dichotomy of existence and non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and objection
Read the first part of the passage: the third point that existence is an existential part and intuitive, and the objection distinguishing conception (tasawwur) and attestation (tasdiq).
Notice the immediate challenge: if intuitive means conception, it is denied; if attestation, then something else is beneficial.
2
Study the first proof
Read the proof from Imam al-Razi: the judgment 'everything is either existent or non-existent' is intuitive and presupposes the conception of existence, so the conception must be intuitive.
This is a classic argument from presupposition: an intuitive judgment's components are more intuitive.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Think about the difference between conception and attestation. The proof aims to show conception is intuitive, but the objection claims only the attestation is intuitive.
This sets up the refutation in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أنه جزء وجودي وهو بديهي، ورد بأنه إن أريد التصور فممنوع أو التصديق فغيره مفيد على ما سبق، وقيل لا يتصور أصلاً. كان الإمام (۱) جعل التصديق ببداهة تصور الوجود كسبياً فاستدل عليه بوجوه . الأول : أن التصديق بأن الوجود والعدم متنافيان، لا يصدقان معاً على أمر أصلاً ، بل كل أمر فإما موجود أو معدوم تصديق بديهي، وهو مسبوق بتصور الوجود والعدم، فهو أولى بالبداهة والجواب أنه إن أريد أن هذا الحكم متعلقاته على ما هو رأي الإمام في التصديق فممنوع، بل مصادرة على المطلوب (۲)، حيث جعل المدعي وهو بداهة تصور الوجود جزءاً من الدليل، وإن أريد أن نفس الحكم بديهي بمعنى أنه لا يتوقف بعد تصور المتعلقات على كسب فمسلم لكنه لا يثبت المدعي وهو بداهة تصور الوجود بحقيقته لجواز الحكم أن يوجد (۳) الحكم البديهي مع عدم تصور الطرفين بالحقيقية بل بوجه ما ومع كون تصورهما كسبياً لا بديهياً، وإنما قلنا في الأول فممنوع بل مصادرة ولم تقتصر على أحدهما تنبيهاً على تمام الجواب بدون بيان المصادرة، وتحقيقاً للزوم المصادرة بأن (٤) بداهة كل جزء من أجزاء هذا التصديق جزء من بداهة هذا التصديق، لأنه لا معنى لبداهة هذا التصديق سوى أن ما يتضمنه من الحكم والطرفين بديهي والعلم بالكل : إما نفس العلم بالأجزاء أو حاصل به على ما مرّ في تصور الماهية وأجزائها، فبالضرورة يكون العلم بكل جزء سابقاً على العلم بالكل لا تابعاً له ممكن الاستفادة منه، ونبطل ما ذكر في المواقف (۱) من أنا نختار أن هذا التصديق بديهي مطلقاً، أي: بجميع أجزائه، ولا مصادرة لأن بداهة هذا التصديق تتوقف على بداهة أجزائه، لكن العلم ببداهته لا يتوقف على العلم ببداهة الأجزاء. فالاستدلال : إنما هو على العلم ببداهة الأجزاء فيجوز أن يستفاد من العلم ببداهة هذا التصديق لأنه يستتبع العلم ببداهة أجزائه بمعنى أنه إذا علم بداهته فكل جزء يلاحظ من أجزائه يعلم أنه بديهي فإن قيل : قد يعقل المركب من غير ملاحظة الأجزاء على التفصيل . قلنا : لو سلم ففي المركب الحقيقي إذ لا معنى لتعقل المركب الاعتباري سوى تعقل الأمور الاعتبارية (۲) المتعددة، التي وضع الاسم بإزائها، ولو سلم ففي التصور للقطع بأنه لا معنى للتصديق ببداهة هذا المركب بجميع أجزائه سوى التصديق بأن هذا الجزء بديهي، وذاك وذاك ولو سلم فلا يلزم المصادرة في شيء من الصور لجواز أن يعلم الدليل مطلقاً من غير توقف على العلم بجزئه الذي هو نفس المذعي
The third: that it is an existential part and it is intuitive. It was responded that if intended conception it is denied, or attestation then other than it is beneficial according to what preceded, and it was said it is not conceivable at all. Imam (1) made the attestation of the intuitiveness of the conception of existence acquired, so he argued for it with proofs. The first: that the attestation that existence and non-existence are mutually exclusive, they are not both true of a thing at all, rather every matter is either existent or non-existent is an intuitive attestation, and it is preceded by the conception of existence and non-existence, so it is more deserving of intuitiveness. The answer: that if it is intended that this judgment and its objects are as the Imam's view on attestation, then it is denied, rather it is a begging of the question (musadarah) of the desired (2), where he made the claim, which is the intuitiveness of the conception of existence, part of the proof. And if it is intended that the judgment itself is intuitive in the sense that it does not depend after the conception of the objects on acquisition, then it is granted but it does not prove the claim, which is the intuitiveness of the conception of existence in its reality, because the intuitive judgment can occur (3) without conception of the two sides in reality but in some manner, and with their conception being acquired not intuitive. And we only said in the first 'it is denied rather it is a begging of the question' and we did not confine ourselves to one of them to indicate the complete answer without explanation of the begging of the question, and to verify the necessity of begging of the question that (4) the intuitiveness of every part of this attestation is part of the intuitiveness of this attestation, because there is no meaning to the intuitiveness of this attestation except that what it includes of judgment and two sides is intuitive, and knowledge of the whole is either the same as knowledge of the parts or obtained by it as previously in the conception of the essence and its parts, so necessarily knowledge of each part is prior to knowledge of the whole, not following it, possibly derivable from it. And we refute what was mentioned in al-Mawaqif (1) that we choose that this attestation is absolutely intuitive, i.e., with all its parts, and there is no begging of the question because the intuitiveness of this attestation depends on the intuitiveness of its parts, but knowledge of its intuitiveness does not depend on knowledge of the intuitiveness of the parts. So the inference is only on knowledge of the intuitiveness of the parts, so it is permissible to derive it from knowledge of the intuitiveness of this attestation because it entails knowledge of the intuitiveness of its parts, meaning that when its intuitiveness is known, then every part observed from its parts is known to be intuitive. For if it is said: a composite may be understood without detailed observation of the parts. We say: if granted, then in the real composite, for there is no meaning for understanding the mental composite except understanding the multiple mental matters (2) for which the name is placed; and if granted, then in the conception, for it is certain that there is no meaning for the attestation of the intuitiveness of this composite with all its parts except the attestation that this part is intuitive, and that, and that; and if granted, then there is no necessary begging of the question in any of the cases because it is possible that the proof is known absolutely without dependence on knowledge of its part which is the very claim.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage presents the third point in a series (likely on the definition of existence) that existence is an existential part and is intuitive (badihi). An objection is raised: if 'intuitive' means conceptualization (tasawwur), it is denied; if it means assent (tasdiq), then something else is beneficial. Then it is reported that Imam al-Razi made the attestation (tasdiq) of the intuitiveness of the conception of existence acquired (kasbi) and argued for it with proofs. The first proof is then given: the attestation that existence and non-existence are mutually exclusive—that every thing is either existent or non-existent—is an intuitive attestation. This attestation is preceded by the conception of existence and non-existence. Therefore, the conception of existence must be even more intuitive (because the presupposition of an intuitive judgment must be at least as intuitive). The response refutes this: if the proof means that the attestation and its objects are as the Imam views attestation (i.e., that the attestation itself is the mental judgment including its terms), then it is a begging of the question (musadarah) because it assumes the intuitiveness of the conception of existence as part of the proof. If instead the proof means that the judgment itself is intuitive in the sense that it does not require acquisition after the concepts are present, then it is granted, but it does not prove that the conception of existence is intuitive in its reality, because an intuitive judgment can occur even if the terms are conceived only in a general manner, not in their true reality, and even if their conception is acquired. The author then refines the response: the first part was called 'denied rather begging of the question' and not merely one of them, to highlight the complete answer without necessitating the explanation of the begging of the question, and to verify the necessity of the begging of the question because the intuitiveness of each part of the attestation is part of the intuitiveness of the attestation itself, since there is no meaning to the intuitiveness of this attestation except that its judgment and terms are intuitive. Knowledge of the whole is either identical to knowledge of the parts or derived from it, as in the conception of a quiddity and its parts. Thus knowledge of each part is necessarily prior to knowledge of the whole, not following it. The author then refutes the view mentioned in al-Mawaqif that the attestation is absolutely intuitive with all its parts, claiming no begging of the question because the intuitiveness of the attestation depends on the intuitiveness of its parts, but knowledge of its intuitiveness does not depend on knowledge of the intuitiveness of the parts. The author argues that the inference is about knowledge of the intuitiveness of the parts, and one could derive that from knowledge of the intuitiveness of the attestation because it entails knowledge of the intuitiveness of its parts (when the whole is known to be intuitive, each part observed is known to be intuitive). Then a further objection is raised: sometimes a composite is understood without detailed observation of parts. The answer: if conceded, it applies only to real composites, not mental composites; for a mental composite (like a concept formed from multiple mental elements) has no meaning apart from understanding its multiple elements. Even if conceded, in the case of a tasawwur (conception) it is certain that attesting to the intuitiveness of such a composite with all its parts is nothing but attesting that each part is intuitive. And even if that is conceded, no circularity arises in any case because it is possible that the proof is known absolutely without dependence on knowledge of its part which is the very claim.

Key Terms
بديهي (badihi)
تصور (tasawwur)
تصديق (tasdiq)
وجود (wujud)
عدم (adam)
كسبي (kasbi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a judgment is intuitive, its terms must also be intuitive in the same way.
Correction: The passage shows that the objection denies that the conception of existence is intuitive, even if the judgment involving it is intuitive.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof for the intuitiveness of the conception of existence?
A) The attestation that existence and non-existence are mutually exclusive is intuitive, and it presupposes the conception of existence, so the conception is intuitive.
B) Existence is self-evident by definition.
C) Imam al-Razi deduced it from the Quran.
D) The concept of existence is innate in all humans.

Key Takeaway
The first proof attempts to establish the intuitiveness of the conception of existence by appealing to an intuitive judgment that presupposes it, but the objection challenges whether conception can be intuitive at all.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever seriously considered whether your understanding of 'existence' is truly intuitive, or have you simply assumed it? If it were not intuitive, what would that do to the foundation of your knowledge of Allah?
?
The proof argues that the judgment 'everything is either existent or non-existent' is self-evident. But is that judgment truly self-evident to you in your daily life, or do you sometimes act as if things are neither one nor the other? Examine your certainty.
?
Imam al-Razi considered the attestation that the conception of existence is intuitive to be acquired, not intuitive. In your own faith, are there truths you hold as acquired rather than self-evident? What proof have you personally relied on?

Rational Judgments
advanced
12 min
Refutation of the First Proof: Begging the Question
Refutes the first proof by showing it involves circular reasoning (musadarah) and discusses the part-whole relation in intuitiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first proof commits begging of the question and how the part-whole relation in intuitiveness is analyzed.

Core Idea
An intuitive judgment does not require intuitive concepts; the proof's structure is circular.

This lesson tackles the sophisticated refutation of al-Razi's proof. The author argues that the proof either assumes what it tries to prove or fails to establish the intended claim. The discussion extends into the nature of composite knowledge and the dependency of whole on parts, culminating in a refutation of a counter-argument from al-Mawaqif.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations
Read the beginning of the answer: 'if it is intended that this judgment and its objects are as the Imam's view... then it is denied, rather it is a begging of the question.' Understand the two possible meanings of the proof.
The author distinguishes between the proof interpreting the judgment as including its objects in a specific way, and the judgment being intuitive in a general sense.
2
Analyze the part-whole argument
Study the section where the author explains why the proof commits musadarah: the intuitiveness of the whole (judgment) depends on the intuitiveness of its parts (including conception of existence). Therefore, using the whole to prove the part is circular.
This is a key logical point: knowledge of the whole is either identical to or derived from knowledge of the parts.
3
Examine the rebuttal of al-Mawaqif
Read the refutation of the view from al-Mawaqif that the attestation is absolutely intuitive and no circularity occurs.
The author argues that even if the whole is known without parts, for a composite of meaning, attesting to its intuitiveness necessarily involves attesting to the intuitiveness of each part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أنه جزء وجودي وهو بديهي، ورد بأنه إن أريد التصور فممنوع أو التصديق فغيره مفيد على ما سبق، وقيل لا يتصور أصلاً. كان الإمام (۱) جعل التصديق ببداهة تصور الوجود كسبياً فاستدل عليه بوجوه . الأول : أن التصديق بأن الوجود والعدم متنافيان، لا يصدقان معاً على أمر أصلاً ، بل كل أمر فإما موجود أو معدوم تصديق بديهي، وهو مسبوق بتصور الوجود والعدم، فهو أولى بالبداهة والجواب أنه إن أريد أن هذا الحكم متعلقاته على ما هو رأي الإمام في التصديق فممنوع، بل مصادرة على المطلوب (۲)، حيث جعل المدعي وهو بداهة تصور الوجود جزءاً من الدليل، وإن أريد أن نفس الحكم بديهي بمعنى أنه لا يتوقف بعد تصور المتعلقات على كسب فمسلم لكنه لا يثبت المدعي وهو بداهة تصور الوجود بحقيقته لجواز الحكم أن يوجد (۳) الحكم البديهي مع عدم تصور الطرفين بالحقيقية بل بوجه ما ومع كون تصورهما كسبياً لا بديهياً، وإنما قلنا في الأول فممنوع بل مصادرة ولم تقتصر على أحدهما تنبيهاً على تمام الجواب بدون بيان المصادرة، وتحقيقاً للزوم المصادرة بأن (٤) بداهة كل جزء من أجزاء هذا التصديق جزء من بداهة هذا التصديق، لأنه لا معنى لبداهة هذا التصديق سوى أن ما يتضمنه من الحكم والطرفين بديهي والعلم بالكل : إما نفس العلم بالأجزاء أو حاصل به على ما مرّ في تصور الماهية وأجزائها، فبالضرورة يكون العلم بكل جزء سابقاً على العلم بالكل لا تابعاً له ممكن الاستفادة منه، ونبطل ما ذكر في المواقف (۱) من أنا نختار أن هذا التصديق بديهي مطلقاً، أي: بجميع أجزائه، ولا مصادرة لأن بداهة هذا التصديق تتوقف على بداهة أجزائه، لكن العلم ببداهته لا يتوقف على العلم ببداهة الأجزاء. فالاستدلال : إنما هو على العلم ببداهة الأجزاء فيجوز أن يستفاد من العلم ببداهة هذا التصديق لأنه يستتبع العلم ببداهة أجزائه بمعنى أنه إذا علم بداهته فكل جزء يلاحظ من أجزائه يعلم أنه بديهي فإن قيل : قد يعقل المركب من غير ملاحظة الأجزاء على التفصيل . قلنا : لو سلم ففي المركب الحقيقي إذ لا معنى لتعقل المركب الاعتباري سوى تعقل الأمور الاعتبارية (۲) المتعددة، التي وضع الاسم بإزائها، ولو سلم ففي التصور للقطع بأنه لا معنى للتصديق ببداهة هذا المركب بجميع أجزائه سوى التصديق بأن هذا الجزء بديهي، وذاك وذاك ولو سلم فلا يلزم المصادرة في شيء من الصور لجواز أن يعلم الدليل مطلقاً من غير توقف على العلم بجزئه الذي هو نفس المذعي
The third: that it is an existential part and it is intuitive. It was responded that if intended conception it is denied, or attestation then other than it is beneficial according to what preceded, and it was said it is not conceivable at all. And we only said in the first 'it is denied rather it is a begging of the question' and we did not confine ourselves to one of them to indicate the complete answer without explanation of the begging of the question, and to verify the necessity of begging of the question that (4) the intuitiveness of every part of this attestation is part of the intuitiveness of this attestation, because there is no meaning to the intuitiveness of this attestation except that what it includes of judgment and two sides is intuitive, and knowledge of the whole is either the same as knowledge of the parts or obtained by it as previously in the conception of the essence and its parts, so necessarily knowledge of each part is prior to knowledge of the whole, not following it, possibly derivable from it. And we refute what was mentioned in al-Mawaqif (1) that we choose that this attestation is absolutely intuitive, i.e., with all its parts, and there is no begging of the question because the intuitiveness of this attestation depends on the intuitiveness of its parts, but knowledge of its intuitiveness does not depend on knowledge of the intuitiveness of the parts. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an intuitive judgment guarantees the intuitiveness of its component concepts.
Correction: The author shows that an intuitive judgment can occur even if the concepts are conceived only in a general or acquired manner, not in their true reality.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument for why the first proof commits begging of the question?
A) The proof assumes the judgment is intuitive without evidence.
B) The intuitiveness of the judgment depends on the intuitiveness of its parts, so using the judgment to prove a part is circular.
C) The proof uses an invalid syllogism.
D) The claim is that existence is not conceivable at all.

Key Takeaway
The first proof fails either by assuming what it needs to prove or by not establishing the required intuitiveness of the conception of existence; the part-whole relation demonstrates the circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed a belief was self-evident (badihi) without examining whether you truly understood its terms? In what area of your faith are you most susceptible to this? Commit to questioning one such assumption this week.
?
The passage shows that circular reasoning can sneak into even sophisticated arguments about God. Are there patterns in your own thinking or in the teachings you accept that might implicitly assume what they set out to prove? Write down one and resolve to trace it back to its foundations.
?
If knowledge of the parts must precede knowledge of the whole, how diligent are you in learning the foundational concepts of your creed (like existence, attributes, actions) before building complex theological positions? Will you dedicate time this week to revisit a foundational concept you might have taken for granted?
