Rational Judgments
beginner
4 min
The Second Proof: Existence Cannot Be Acquired by Definition
Presents the second proof that existence cannot be known through definition because it is simple.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence cannot be acquired by definition (hadd) due to its simplicity and the logical absurdities that would follow if it were composite.

Core Idea
Existence is too simple to be defined; defining it leads to logical absurdities.

This lesson examines the second proof from Sanusi demonstrating that the conception of existence is intuitive (badihi). The argument proceeds by showing that existence cannot be acquired through definition (hadd), because definition is only for composite things, yet existence is absolutely simple. By understanding this proof, you will grasp a key rationale for why existence is known necessarily and not through conceptual analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to follow the argument's flow.
Notice the concise, logical style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the main claim and premises
The passage starts by stating that existence is known in its reality. Then it outlines the ways knowledge is acquired: necessarily (daruri) or by acquisition (kasbi), and the latter through hadd or rasm. This sets up the proof.
The argument is addressed to those who accept these premises.
3
Understand the core argument against hadd
Hadd is only for composite things. Existence is not composite. If it were composite, its parts would be either existences or something else. Both lead to absurdities, as explained.
Focus on the two absurd consequences: priority of part over whole and equality of part with whole.
4
Reflect on why this matters
This proof shows that existence cannot be defined, so it must be known directly (daruri). This is foundational for subsequent theological arguments.
Consider how this relates to the broader topic of God's existence being self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : إن الوجود معلوم بحقيقته وحصول العلم إما بالضرورة أو الاكتساب وطريق الاكتساب إما الحد أو الرسم، وهذا احتجاج على من يعترف بهذه المقدمات، فلهذا لم يتعرض لمنعها والوجود يمتنع اكتسابه أما بالحد فلأنه إنما يكون للمركب، والوجود ليس بمركب، وإلا فأجزاؤه إما وجودات أو غيرها فإن كانت وجودات لزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه ومساواة الجزء للكل في تمام ماهيته وكلاهما محال .
The second aspect: that existence is known in its reality, and attainment of knowledge is either by necessity or acquisition, and the way of acquisition is either by definition (hadd) or description (rasm). This is an argument against one who acknowledges these premises, so for this reason it did not address their denial. Existence is prevented from acquisition, as for definition (hadd) because it is only for the composite, and existence is not composite; otherwise its parts would be either existences or other than them. If they are existences, then there would follow the thing's priority over itself and the equality of the part with the whole in the entirety of its quiddity, and both are impossible.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the badihah (intuitiveness) of the conception of existence. It begins by stating that existence is known in its reality. Knowledge is either necessary (daruri, i.e., intuitive) or acquired (kasbi). The way of acquisition is either by hadd (definition per genus and differentia) or rasm (description). This argument is addressed to someone who acknowledges these premises, so the author does not discuss their denial here. The proof then argues that existence cannot be acquired by hadd because hadd applies only to composite things (to analyze them into genus and differentia), and existence is not composite. If it were composite, its parts would be either existences or something else. If they are existences, then that would entail the thing's priority over itself (since the part would be prior to the whole, but the part is also an existence of the same kind) and the part's equality with the whole in its complete quiddity (since each part would be a complete existence, making the whole redundant). Both consequences are impossible. Thus existence cannot be defined by hadd; the proof continues (in subsequent text not included) to also address rasm and conclude that it must be known by necessity.

Key Terms
حد (hadd)
رسم (rasm)
مركب (murakkab)
وجود (wujud)
ضروري (daruri)
كسبي (kasbi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that existence can be defined like other concepts, using genus and differentia.
Correction: This proof shows that existence is too simple to be defined. Any attempt to define it leads to absurdities, indicating it must be known directly without definition.

Checkpoint
Why does the second proof hold that existence cannot be acquired by hadd?
A) Because hadd applies only to necessary beings.
B) Because existence is composite and would require prior existence.
C) Because existence is not composite, and if it were, its composition would lead to absurdities.
D) Because hadd is only for mental concepts.

Key Takeaway
Existence cannot be acquired by definition (hadd) because it is simple and any attempt to define it leads to logical absurdities such as self-priority and part-whole equivalence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence is so simple that it cannot be defined, then your own existence is not a concept you acquired—it is a direct reality from Allah. When did you last pause to recognize that your very being is a gift you did not earn and cannot fully comprehend? How would your day look if you started it with that awareness?
?
The proof shows that trying to define existence leads to absurdity. In your life, do you catch yourself defining your worth by categories like job, status, or achievements—as if existence itself were not enough? What would it mean to stop defining yourself and simply rest in the fact that you exist before Allah?
?
Existence is known necessarily, without effort or acquisition. Yet how often do you take your own existence for granted, forgetting that it is a direct sign of Allah's power? What one practice could you begin today to remind yourself constantly that your existence is a gift from Him?

Third Way: Absolute Existence and Its Refutation
Sanusi presents a third argument and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that absolute existence is self-evident as part of individual existence and its refutation through distinguishing conceptualization from assent.

Core Idea
Absolute existence is not a part of individual existence; it is an external necessary concomitant.

This passage presents a third argument for the self-evidence of absolute existence, claiming it is a part of one's individual existence. The author refutes this by carefully separating two different claims: one about conceptualization and one about assent. Understanding this refutation is crucial for grasping how kalam distinguishes between constitutive parts and external attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and refutation
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the flow of the argument. Note that the passage begins with the third way (الوجه الثالث) and then gives the answer (والجواب).
The passage ends abruptly; we focus on the available text.
2
Identify the two parts of the refutation
The answer addresses two interpretations of 'self-evident': one concerning conceptualization (تصور) of my existence, and another concerning assent (تصديق) that I exist. Locate where each begins.
The transition is marked by 'وإن أريد' for the second part.
3
Understand the key distinction
The refutation hinges on the concept of absolute existence being a 'lāzim khārijī' (external necessary concomitant), not a part. An accident cannot be a part of its subject. Also, self-evidence of individual existence does not ensure self-evidence of absolute existence.
This distinction is central to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث: أن الوجود المطلق جزء من وجودي لأن معناه الوجود مع الإضافة، والعلم بوجودي بديهي، بمعنى أنه لا يتوقف على كسب أصلاً فيكون الوجود المطلق بديهياً، لأن ما يتوقف عليه البديهي يكون بديهياً. والجواب: أنه إن أريد أن تصور وجودي بالحقيقة بديهي فممنوع، ولو سلم فلا نسلم أن المطلق جزء منه أو تصوره جزء من تصوره، لما سيجيء من أن الوجود المطلق يقع على الوجودات وقوع لازم خارجي غير مقوم، وليس العارض جزءاً للمعروض ولا تصوره لتصوره. وإن أريد أن التصديق أي العلم بأني موجود ضروري فغير مفيد لأن كونه بديهياً لجميع الأجزاء غير مسلم، وكون حكمه بديهياً غير مستلزم لتصور الطرفين بالحقيقة، فضلاً وظاهر تقرير الإمام بل صريحه أن المراد هو تصديق الإنسان بأنه موجود ثم
The third way: that absolute existence is a part of my existence (wujūdī) because its meaning is existence with addition, and knowledge of my existence is self-evident, in the sense that it does not depend on any acquisition at all, so absolute existence would be self-evident, because that on which the self-evident depends is self-evident. And the answer: If it is meant that the conceptualization of my existence in reality is self-evident, it is denied; and even if granted, we do not concede that the absolute is a part of it or that its conceptualization is part of its conceptualization, because it will later be shown that absolute existence applies to existences as an external necessary concomitant, not constitutive; and the accident is not a part of its subject, nor its conceptualization part of its conceptualization. And if it is meant that the assent, i.e., the knowledge that I exist, is necessary, then it is not useful because its being self-evident for all parts is not granted, and its judgment being self-evident does not entail the conceptualization of the two extremes in reality, especially since the explicit statement of the Imam (al-Rāzī) is that what is intended is the assent of a person that he exists, then
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument (الوجه الثالث) that absolute existence is part of individual existence (wujūdī) and thus self-evident. The author refutes it in two ways. First, if the claim is that the conceptualization (taṣawwur) of my existence is self-evident, it is denied; even if granted, absolute existence is not a part (juzʾ) of individual existence but an external necessary concomitant (lāzim khārijī). The accident is not a part of the subject. Second, if the claim is about assent (taṣdīq) that I exist is necessary, that does not help because the self-evidence of individual existence does not guarantee the self-evidence of absolute existence for all, and self-evident judgment does not require true conceptualization of the terms. The author references Imam al-Rāzī's position that what is meant is the assent of a person that he exists.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If individual existence is self-evident, then absolute existence must also be self-evident because it is a part of individual existence.
Correction: Absolute existence is not a constitutive part but an external necessary concomitant (lāzim khārijī). An accident is not part of its subject, so self-evidence of the subject does not transfer to the accident.

The self-evidence of individual existence does not imply the self-evidence of absolute existence, because absolute existence is an accident, not a part.

Refuting the Claim that Absolute Existence is Self-Evident
Presents and refutes the third argument for absolute existence's self-evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why absolute existence cannot be considered self-evident simply because it is thought to be part of one's own existence, and how the refutation distinguishes between conception and assent.

Core Idea
Absolute existence is a concomitant, not a part of individual existence.

This passage tackles a subtle argument that absolute existence is self-evident because it is said to be part of 'my existence', which is intuitively known. The refutation is carefully layered, addressing misconceptions about what it means for something to be a 'part' and clarifying the difference between conceiving something and assenting to its existence. By working through this argument, you will sharpen your understanding of how self-evidence works in rational theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument for absolute existence
Start by reading the first part of the Arabic passage, which presents the third argument: absolute existence is a part of 'my existence', and since knowledge of my existence is self-evident, absolute existence must also be self-evident.
Notice the claim that the self-evident depends on that which is also self-evident.
2
Analyze the first prong of the refutation (conception)
The author denies that the conception of 'my existence' is self-evident. Even if it were, absolute existence is not a part but an external concomitant (lāzim khārijī) – an accident, not a constitutive part. Conception of the accident is not part of conception of the substrate.
Focus on the distinction between a part (juz') and a concomitant (lāzim).
3
Analyze the second prong (assent)
If the claim is about assent (taṣdīq) – that I know I exist – this also fails. Self-evidence of judgment does not require full conception of the terms. The author reports al-Rāzī's position and the objection against it.
Note the shift from conception to assent.
4
Understand the refinement about proof
The later part discusses the nature of proof: what is necessary is a necessary proof itself, not the external existence of its premises. Also, an affirmative proposition judges the truth of the predicate for the subject, which is existence itself. This clarifies the role of proof in reaching conception and assent.
This is a meta-discussion about proof structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث: أن الوجود المطلق جزء من وجودي لأن معناه الوجود مع الإضافة، والعلم بوجودي بديهي، بمعنى أنه لا يتوقف على كسب أصلاً فيكون الوجود المطلق بديهياً، لأن ما يتوقف عليه البديهي يكون بديهياً. والجواب: أنه إن أريد أن تصور وجودي بالحقيقة بديهي فممنوع، ولو سلم فلا نسلم أن المطلق جزء منه أو تصوره جزء من تصوره، لما سيجيء من أن الوجود المطلق يقع على الوجودات وقوع لازم خارجي غير مقوم، وليس العارض جزءاً للمعروض ولا تصوره لتصوره. وإن أريد أن التصديق أي العلم بأني موجود ضروري فغير مفيد لأن كونه بديهياً لجميع الأجزاء غير مسلم، وكون حكمه بديهياً غير مستلزم لتصور الطرفين بالحقيقة، فضلاً وظاهر تقرير الإمام بل صريحه أن المراد هو تصديق الإنسان بأنه موجود ثم أورد منع بداهته، فأجاب بأنه على تقدير كونه كسبياً لا بد من الانتهاء إلى دليل يعلم وجوده بالضرورة قطعاً للتسلسل، والعلم بالوجود جزء من ذلك العلم فيكون ضرورياً. وصرح صاحب المواقف بأنه جزء وجودي وهو متصور بالبديهة، ثم أورد جواب الإمام عن منع المذكور وزاد عليه. فقال: وأيضاً لا دليل عن سالبتين فلا بد من الانتهاء إلى موجبة يحكم فيها بوجود المحمول للموضوع ضرورة، ثم دفعهما بأن الذي لا بد من الانتهاء إليه دليل هو ضروري لا وجوده، فإنا نستدل بصدق المقدمتين لا بوجودهما في الخارج وبأن الموجبة ما حكم فيها بصدق المحمول على ما صدق عليه الموضوع لا بوجوده له، وأنت خبير بأنه لا دخل للدليل وترتيب المقدمتين في الإيصال إلى التصور، وإن كان كلامه صريحاً في أنه يريد بالدليل الموصل إلى التصديق لا الموصل في الجملة وأن مراد الإمام بالدليل الذي لا بد من العلم بوجوده هو الأمر الذي يستدل به، كالعالم للصانع لا المقدمات المرتبة وأنه لا معنى لصدق المحمول على الموضوع سوى وجوده له، وثبوته له.
The third aspect: that absolute existence is a part of 'my existence' (wujūdī) because its meaning is existence with an addition, and knowledge of my existence is self-evident (badīhī), meaning it does not depend on acquisition at all; thus absolute existence is self-evident, because that on which the self-evident depends is self-evident. And the answer: if it is intended that the conception (taṣawwur) of my existence in reality is self-evident, it is denied; and even if granted, we do not concede that the absolute is a part of it or its conception is part of its conception, because of what will come: that absolute existence occurs over existences as an external concomitant (lāzim khārijī) that is not constitutive (muqawwim), and the accident (ʿāriḍ) is not a part of the substrate (maʿrūḍ) nor is its conception part of its conception. And if it is intended that the assent (taṣdīq), i.e., the knowledge that I exist, is necessary, it is not useful, because its being self-evident for all parts is not conceded, and its judgment being self-evident does not entail the conception of the two extremes in reality, especially since the explicit presentation of the Imam—rather his clear statement—is that what is meant is the assent of a person that he exists. Then he objected to its self-evidence, and answered that on the assumption that it is acquired, one must end with a proof whose existence is known necessarily to avoid infinite regress, and knowledge of existence is part of that knowledge, so it is necessary. And the author of al-Mawāqif stated that it is an existential part and is conceived by intuition (badīha). Then he presented the Imam's answer to the mentioned objection and added to it. He said: Moreover, there is no proof from two negative propositions; thus one must end with an affirmative proposition in which the existence of the predicate for the subject is judged necessarily. Then he refuted them by saying that what one must end with is a proof that is necessary, not its existence; for we argue by the truth of the two premises, not by their existence externally, and that the affirmative proposition is one in which the truth of the predicate for what the subject is true of is judged, not its existence for it. And you are aware that the proof and arrangement of premises have no role in reaching conception; although his statement is explicit that he means by 'proof' that which leads to assent, not a proof in general, and that the Imam meant by 'proof whose existence must be known' the thing used as evidence, like the world for the Maker, not the arranged premises, and that there is no meaning to 'truth of the predicate for the subject' other than its existence for it and its being established for it.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the third argument that absolute existence is self-evident because it is part of 'my existence' (wujūdī), which is self-evident. The author first states the argument: 'my existence' means existence with an addition, and knowledge of my existence is intuitive, so absolute existence must be intuitive too. The refutation is detailed: First, the claim that the conception of my existence is self-evident is denied. Even if granted, absolute existence is not a part of my existence but an external concomitant (lāzim khārijī) that is not constitutive; an accident is not a part of its substrate. Second, if what is meant is the assent (taṣdīq) that I exist, that does not help because its self-evidence for all parts is not conceded, and a self-evident judgment does not entail full conception of its terms. The author then reports the Imam's (al-Rāzī) position that the intended self-evidence is of the assent that one exists, but then an objection is raised against its self-evidence. The Imam answers that even if it were acquired, it must ultimately depend on a necessarily known proof to avoid infinite regress, and knowledge of existence is part of that proof, making existence necessary. The author of al-Mawāqif adds that this necessary existential part is conceived intuitively. He then refines the objection: a proof cannot consist of two negative propositions; it must end with an affirmative proposition asserting existence. But the reply is that what is necessary is the proof itself, not the existence of its premises; we rely on the truth of premises, not their external existence. The predicate's truth for the subject is precisely its existence for it. The passage ends by clarifying that the proof leading to conception is not the same as that leading to assent.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is a part of a self-evident whole, then that something is also self-evident.
Correction: The passage clarifies that absolute existence is not a part but an external concomitant (lāzim khārijī), which is accidental and not constitutive. An accident's conception is not part of the conception of its substrate.

Checkpoint
Why does the author deny that absolute existence is a part of 'my existence'?

C) Because absolute existence is more universal.

Key Takeaway
Absolute existence is a non-constitutive concomitant of individual existences, not a part, so its self-evidence cannot be derived from the self-evidence of 'my existence'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would it mean for you if even your most basic self-knowledge rests on assumptions you cannot fully articulate?
?
This lesson argues that absolute existence is not a part of your existence but a concomitant. Think of something you consider essential to your identity – is it possible that what you take to be a part of you is actually an accidental concomitant? Are you prepared to reexamine that belief?
?
The refutation shows that a proof is needed to avoid infinite regress. In your own life, where do you rely on something being 'self-evident' without examining it? Will you allow this lesson to push you to uncover the hidden premises behind your deepest convictions, even if it is uncomfortable?

Objection: Existence as Identical to Essence or Accidental
Raises an objection about whether existence is identical to essence or an accident

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objections raised against the claim that existence is either identical to essence or an accident, and why the objector argues this does not solve the problem of dependence.

Core Idea
Existence cannot be simply identical to essence or an accident without leading to logical difficulties.

This passage presents a sharp objection against the view that existence is either identical to essence or an accident. The objector argues that both options fail to avoid dependence, and that the appeal to 'absolute existence' only worsens the issue. Additionally, the objection claims that if existence were self-evident, scholars would not have debated its definition or provided proofs. We will unpack this objection step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the structure: the author first notes a possible response, then the objection begins with 'فإن قيل'.
The objection is introduced with 'فإن قيل' which is a classic form for presenting a counterargument.
2
Identify the two main arguments in the objection
The objection contains two distinct arguments: (1) existence is either identical to essence or an accident, both leading to dependence; (2) if existence were self-evident, rational people would not define it or disagree. Locate these in the text.
The second argument is introduced by 'وأيضاً' meaning 'also'.
3
Understand the dependence argument
If existence is identical to essence, it is acquired like essence. If it is an accident, it is only conceivable as dependent on essence. The objector says 'absolute existence' as a substrate does not remove dependence but increases it. Reflect on why this is problematic.
4
Understand the self-evidence argument
If existence were self-evident (badīhī), then defining it and debating its self-evidence would be unnecessary. But in fact, scholars have done so, indicating it is not self-evident. Consider what this implies for the discussion.
This argument targets the idea that existence is a primitive concept.
5
Reflect on the objection's force
Think about how this objection challenges the standard classification of existence as either identical to essence or an accident. Why might this be a serious problem for the author's earlier claims?
The response to this objection is not in this passage, so we are left with an open question.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم يتجه أن يقال: الوجود هنا رابطة، وليس الكلام فيه الوجود نفس الماهية أو زائد عليها. قال: فإن قيل: هو إما نفس الماهية فيكتسب مثلها، أو عارض فلا يعقل إلا تبعاً لها، والقول بأن الكلام في مطلق الوجود أو المعروض مطلق الماهية لا يدفع التبعية بل يزيدها، وأيضاً لو كان بديهياً لم يشتغل العقلاء بتعريفه، ولم يختلفوا في بداهته، ولم يحتجوا عليها.
Yes, it is correct to say: existence here is a copula (rābiṭa), and the discussion is not about existence being identical to essence or additional to it. He said: For if it is said: it is either the essence itself, so it is acquired like it, or an accident (ʿāriḍ), so it is not conceivable except as dependent on it; and the statement that the discussion is about absolute existence or the substrate is absolute essence does not remove dependence but rather increases it. Also, if it were self-evident, the intelligent would not have occupied themselves with its definition, nor would they have disagreed about its self-evidence, nor would they have needed proofs for it.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage presents a new objection regarding the nature of existence. The author first notes that one might correctly say that existence here functions as a copula (rābiṭa), and the discussion is not about whether existence is identical to essence or additional. Then an objector argues: existence is either identical to essence, in which case it is acquired like essence, or it is an accident (ʿāriḍ), in which case it is inconceivable except as dependent on essence. The counterargument that the discussion concerns absolute existence or the substrate as absolute essence does not eliminate the dependence but rather increases it. Furthermore, if existence were self-evident, rational people would not have busied themselves defining it, disagreed about its self-evidence, or needed proofs for it. This objection remains unanswered in the provided text.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a concept you assume is self-evident (like existence), are you willing to examine it critically, or do you resist because it feels too basic? What assumptions about your own faith or life do you treat as self-evident without ever testing them?

The fact that scholars debated existence shows its profundity. What profound questions about your own existence have you been avoiding because they seem too difficult? Will you commit to reflecting on one such question this week?

The Necessity of a Self-Evident Premise in Proving Existence
Argument that denying self-evidence leads to infinite regress and requires an affirmative necessary premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying the self-evidence of existence leads to an infinite regress and how the argument requires an affirmative, self-evident premise.

Core Idea
Denying the obvious forces an endless chase — proof must start from the self-evident.

This passage tackles a fundamental objection: if existence is not self-evident, how can we know it? The argument shows that any chain of reasoning must end at a premise known by necessity, and existence is part of that necessary knowledge. The author works through objections and clarifications to establish that the ultimate proof is the evidence itself, not its external existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the dialectical structure: objection, response, further objection, and refutation.
2
Follow the infinite regress argument
The initial objection denies that existence is self-evident. The response argues that if it were acquired, infinite regress would result, so one must end at a proof whose existence is known necessarily.
Identify the key terms: بداهة (self-evidence), كسبي (acquired), تسلسل (infinite regress).
3
Understand the need for an affirmative premise
The author introduces the Imam's claim: no proof from two negative premises; one must end at an affirmative premise that judges the existence of the predicate for the subject.
Contrast سالبتين (two negatives) with موجبة (affirmative).
4
Grasp the refutation about the proof's existence
The opponent refutes that what is necessary is the proof itself, not its existence. We infer from the truth of premises, not their external existence.
Notice the distinction between دليل (proof/evidence) and وجوده (its existence).
5
Note the final clarification
The author clarifies that 'existence' here functions as a copula, not as a property of essence. This sets the stage for further discussion.
This clarification avoids confusion with the later debate on whether existence is identical to essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أورد منع بداهته، فأجاب بأنه على تقدير كونه كسبياً لا بد من الانتهاء إلى دليل يعلم وجوده بالضرورة قطعاً للتسلسل، والعلم بالوجود جزء من ذلك العلم فيكون ضرورياً. وصرح صاحب المواقف بأنه جزء وجودي وهو متصور بالبديهة، ثم أورد جواب الإمام عن منع المذكور وزاد عليه. فقال: وأيضاً لا دليل عن سالبتين فلا بد من الانتهاء إلى موجبة يحكم فيها بوجود المحمول للموضوع ضرورة، ثم دفعهما بأن الذي لا بد من الانتهاء إليه دليل هو ضروري لا وجوده، فإنا نستدل بصدق المقدمتين لا بوجودهما في الخارج وبأن الموجبة ما حكم فيها بصدق المحمول على ما صدق عليه الموضوع لا بوجوده له، وأنت خبير بأنه لا دخل للدليل وترتيب المقدمتين في الإيصال إلى التصور، وإن كان كلامه صريحاً في أنه يريد بالدليل الموصل إلى التصديق لا الموصل في الجملة وأن مراد الإمام بالدليل الذي لا بد من العلم بوجوده هو الأمر الذي يستدل به، كالعالم للصانع لا المقدمات المرتبة وأنه لا معنى لصدق المحمول على الموضوع سوى وجوده له، وثبوته له. نعم يتجه أن يقال: الوجود هنا رابطة، وليس الكلام فيه الوجود نفس الماهية أو زائد عليها.
He adduced the denial of its self-evidence, and answered that on the supposition of its being acquired (kashiy), one must end at a proof whose existence is known necessarily to cut off infinite regress, and knowledge of existence is part of that knowledge, so it is necessary. And the author of al-Mawaqif stated that it is an existential part and is conceived intuitively (badihah). Then he adduced the Imam's answer to the mentioned denial and added to it. He said: Also, there is no proof from two negative premises, so one must end at an affirmative premise in which the existence of the predicate for the subject is judged necessarily. Then he refuted both: that what one must end at is a necessary proof, not its existence; for we infer from the truth of the two premises, not from their existence in the external world; and that the affirmative premise is that in which the truth of the predicate for what the subject is true of is judged, not its existence for it. And you are aware that the proof and the arrangement of the premises have no role in reaching the conception [of existence], although his words are explicit in that he means by 'proof' that which leads to assent, not that which leads in general; and that the Imam intended by 'proof whose existence must be known' the thing that is used as evidence, like the world for the Maker, not the arranged premises; and that there is no meaning of the truth of the predicate for the subject other than its existence for it and its being established for it. Yes, it may be said: existence here is a copula, and the discussion is not about whether existence is identical to essence or additional to it.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation of the denial of the self-evidence of existence. The author begins by presenting an objection: if existence were not self-evident but acquired, an infinite regress would follow. The answer is that one must eventually arrive at a premise whose existence is known by necessity, and since knowledge of existence is part of that knowledge, existence itself becomes necessary. The author of al-Mawaqif confirms that existence is an existential part (juz' wujudi) and is conceived intuitively. Then the author introduces the Imam's response: no inference can be made from two negative premises; one must reach an affirmative premise that affirms the existence of the predicate for the subject. The opponent's refutation is then addressed: what is necessary is the proof itself, not its existence, because we infer from the truth of premises, not from their external existence. Also, an affirmative premise only requires the truth of the predicate for the subject's referent, not its existence for the subject. The author clarifies that the proof and arrangement of premises do not affect conception; the 'proof' meant by the Imam is the evidence (e.g., the world for the Maker), not the structured syllogism. Finally, the author notes that existence here functions as a copula, not as either essential or accidental, setting the stage for the subsequent objections.

Correction: The passage clarifies that what is necessary is the proof itself (the evidence) known necessarily, not its external existence. We infer from the truth of premises, not their external existence.

Checkpoint

Objection: Existence Is Either Essential or Accidental
Objection-and-answer on the self-evidence of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that existence cannot be self-evident because it is either identical to essence or a non-essential attribute, and the rebuttal that dependency does not preclude self-evidence.

Core Idea
Existence's dependency does not negate its self-evidence.

This short but dense passage presents a powerful objection against the self-evidence of existence and its rebuttal. The objection uses a dichotomy: existence is either the same as essence or an accident. Both options seem to lead to non-self-evidence. But the answer challenges this logic by showing that dependency does not entail non-self-evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the compact style and the logical connectors.
The passage is an objection and answer; identify the turning point 'قلنا'.
2
Analyze the objection's dichotomy
The objector presents two possibilities: existence is either identical to essence (nifs al-māhiyya) or a non-essential attribute (ʻāriḍ). For each, explain why the objector thinks it cannot be self-evident.
If existence is identical to essence, it gains the essence's non-self-evidence; if it is an accident, it depends on essence.
3
Understand the two-part answer
The answer (قلنا) gives two responses: first, an accident may take the role of the subject and be conceived independently; second, even if dependency is granted, the modality of essence is self-evident, so existence can be self-evident too.
This rebuttal reframes the relationship between existence and essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : فإن قيل : هو إما نفس الماهية فيكتسب مثلها ، أو عارض فلا يعقل إلا تبعاً لها، والقول بأن الكلام في مطلق الوجود أو المعروض مطلق الماهية لا يدفع التبعية بل يزيدها. قلنا: قد يعقل العارض دور المعروض، ولو سلم فكيفي ماهية بديهية.
He said: If it is said: It (existence) is either identical to essence, then it is acquired like it; or a non-essential attribute, then it is not intelligible except as dependent on it. And the claim that the discussion is about absolute existence or that the subject is absolute essence does not remove the dependency but increases it. We said: The non-essential attribute may take the role of the subject, and even if granted, its modality is a self-evident essence.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer (objection-and-answer) concerning the self-evidence of existence. The objection presents a dichotomy: existence is either identical to essence (māhiyya) or a non-essential attribute (‘āriḍ). If identical, then existence, like the essence, is not self-evident (since essences are commonly not self-evident). If a non-essential attribute, it can only be conceived as dependent on its subject (the essence), and since essences are not self-evident, existence cannot be either. The objection also dismisses the possibility that the discussion concerns absolute existence (wujūd muṭlaq) or absolute essence, arguing that such abstraction does not remove dependency but deepens it. The answer (قلنا) is twofold: first, a non-essential attribute may actually take the role of the subject (i.e., be conceived independently), meaning dependency does not preclude self-evidence. Second, even if dependency is accepted, the modality (kaifiyya) of the essence is itself self-evident, so existence as a non-essential attribute can also be self-evident.

عارض
مطلق الوجود
تبعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is dependent on something else, it cannot be self-evident.
Correction: Dependency does not automatically make something non-self-evident; an attribute can be self-evident even if it depends on a subject, as shown in the answer.

Checkpoint
What is the objector's claim about absolute existence?
A) It is self-evident
B) It removes dependency between existence and essence
C) It increases dependency instead of removing it
D) It is the same as specific existence

Key Takeaway
Existence can be self-evident even if it is an accident of essence, because dependency does not negate self-evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The objector argues that if existence is accidental, it cannot be known independently. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes treat the existence of God as a derivative conclusion from arguments rather than a self-evident truth? What would it mean for you to worship a God whose existence is as obvious as your own?
?
Think of a time you doubted a fundamental truth about yourself or your faith. Did your doubt itself rely on the existence of the very thing you doubted? How does this passage challenge you to ground your certainty not in intellectual constructs but in the immediate recognition of what is manifest?

Objection: Defining Existence Contradicts Its Self-Evidence
Refutes the claim that definition of existence negates its self-evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining existence does not negate its self-evidence, as the definition clarifies the intended meaning of the word rather than the concept itself.

Core Idea
Defining a self-evident term explains the word, not the concept.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection: if existence is self-evident, why do scholars define it? The answer distinguishes between clarifying the meaning of a word and conceiving the reality itself. This distinction preserves the self-evident nature of existence while explaining the need for definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the objection and the answer (قلنا).
The passage is cut off at the end, but the core argument is complete.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states that if existence were self-evident, rational people would not define it, disagree on its self-evidence, or need proof.
3
Understand the answer
The answer explains that defining a self-evident term can mean clarifying the intended meaning of the word, not the conception of the reality itself. Also, assent to self-evidence may be acquired or obscure, leading to differences.
This distinguishes between the concept and the term.
4
Note the two arguments of deniers
The author summarizes two arguments: (1) existence is either identical to essence or an accident; (2) the definition objection we just read. Both are answered by the same distinction.
The first argument is referenced from earlier.
5
Reflect on the key distinction
Consider that defining a word does not imply the concept is unknown; it specifies which meaning is intended among possible ambiguities.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً لو كان بديهياً لم يشتغل العقلاء بتعريفه، ولم يختلفوا في بداهته، ولم يحتجوا عليها. قلنا: قد يفسر البديهي لفظ الإفادة المراد باللفظ لا تصور الحقيقة، وقد يكون التصديق ببداهة البديهي كسبياً أو خفياً فيختلف فيه ويفتقر إلى الدليل أو التنبيه. يريد أن يشير إلى تمسكات المنكرين ببداهة الوجود، مع الجواب عنها. وهي وجوه الأول: ما تقدم من أنه إما نفس الماهية أو عارض. الثاني: أن الوجود لو كان بديهياً لم يشتغل العقلاء بتعريفه، كما لم يشتغلوا بإقامة البرهان على القضايا البديهية، لكونهم عرفوه بوجهه كما.
Also, if it were self-evident, rational people would not have busied themselves with defining it, nor would they have differed about its self-evidence, nor would they have needed proof for it. We said: The self-evident might be explained by [stating] the meaning intended by the word, not the conception of the reality; and assent to the self-evidence of the self-evident may be acquired or obscure, so they differ about it and need proof or alerting. He intends to indicate the arguments of the deniers of the self-evidence of existence, along with the answers to them. They are aspects: The first: what preceded, that it is either identical to essence or non-essential. The second: that if existence were self-evident, rational people would not have busied themselves with defining it, just as they did not busy themselves with establishing proof for self-evident propositions, because they knew it in its proper manner, as.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer regarding the definition of existence. The objection argues that if existence were self-evident (badīhī), rational people would not have put effort into defining it, nor would there be disagreement about its self-evidence, nor would they need proof for it. The answer (قلنا) is that the definition of a self-evident term may serve to clarify the intended meaning of the word, not to conceive the reality itself (the concept may already be known). Furthermore, the assent (taṣdīq) that a certain concept is self-evident may itself be acquired (kashī) or obscure, leading to differences and requiring proof or alerting. Then the author summarizes this as one of the two main arguments (wujūh) of the deniers: the second objection specifically about definition; the first was the essence-attribute objection. He notes that people define existence not because the concept is unknown but to specify which meaning is intended, analogous to defining a term whose reality is obvious but the term is ambiguous.

Correction: Defining a self-evident term does not disprove its self-evidence; it clarifies the word's intended meaning, not the concept itself.

The Accident Can Be Known Without Its Subject
The author states the principle that accidents are known without their subjects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that an accidental attribute can be conceived independently of its subject and how this relates to the self-evidentness of existence.

Core Idea
An accident's meaning does not depend on knowing its subject.

This lesson begins by tackling a key objection: if existence is an accident of essence, how can existence be self-evident? The author argues that accidents can indeed be known without their subjects, establishing a crucial logical principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the first lines of the Arabic and its translation. Notice the author's assertive opening 'We say' and the principle stated.
The passage is from a response to an objection.
2
Understand the principle
The author states that the accident (al-ʿāriḍ) may be known without the subject (al-maʿrūḍ). Consider what this implies for existence as an accident of essence.
This is a logical move to defend self-evidence.
3
Apply to the objection
Reflect on how this principle answers the objection that existence cannot be self-evident if it is an accident. The author then poses a rhetorical question to set up further discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : قد يعقل العارض دور المعروض، ولو سلم فكيفي ماهية بديهية، وقد يفسر البديهي لفظ الإفادة المراد باللفظ لا تصور الحقيقة، وقد يكون التصديق ببداهة البديهي كسبياً أو خفياً فيختلف فيه (١) ويفتقر إلى الدليل أو التنبيه.
We say: The accident may be known without the subject, and if it is granted, then how is it that an essence is self-evident? And the self-evident may be explained by a verbal definition indicating the intended meaning of the word, not the conception of the reality. And the assent to the self-evidentness of the self-evident may be acquired or hidden, thus differing concerning it (1) and needing evidence or alerting.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is an opening argument by the author. He begins by stating a key principle: an accidental attribute (al-ʿāriḍ) can be conceived without its subject (al-maʿrūḍ). This is a direct response to the claim that if existence is an accident of essence, it cannot be self-evident because accidents depend on their subjects. He then addresses a potential concession: even if one concedes that existence is an accident, how does that affect the self-evidentness of essence? He then shifts to a discussion of definition. The self-evident (al-badīhī) can be defined, but only by a nominal definition (taʿrīf lafẓī) that clarifies what is meant by the word, not a real definition that grants conceptualization of the essence. Finally, he notes that the assent (al-taṣdīq) to the self-evidentness of a self-evident matter can be acquired (kasbī) or hidden (khafī), which is why people may differ about it and need evidence or alerting. This anticipates the objections that follow.

Key Terms
العارض
المعروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It requires knowledge of the subject.
B) It may be known without the subject.
C) It is identical to the subject.
D) It cannot be defined.

Key Takeaway
The principle that accidents are knowable without their subjects is essential for defending the self-evidentness of existence.

The First Objection: Existence as Essence or Accident
First objection with two-horned dilemma and sub-argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that existence is either identical to essence or an accident, and why both options seem to deny its self-evidentness.

Core Idea
Existence must be either essence or accident—both seemingly incompatible with self-evidence.

This lesson presents the first objection raised against the self-evidentness of existence. The objector argues that existence must be either the same as essence or an addition to it; in either case, it cannot be self-evident. Understanding this dilemma is crucial to appreciating the subsequent refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection's opening dilemma
Start by reading the first few lines where the objector presents the two possibilities: existence is either identical to essence or additional to it.
Notice the forced choice between two options.
2
Analyze the first horn
If existence is identical to essence, and essences are not self-evident, then existence would also be non-self-evident. Consider why this follows.
The objector assumes essences are acquired, not self-evident.
3
Analyze the second horn
If existence is additional, it is an accident. An accident depends on its subject for intelligibility, so existence would be dependent on a non-self-evident subject, thus not self-evident.
Accident's dependency is key.
4
Consider the sub-argument about absolute vs particular existence
The objector extends the argument to absolute existence, claiming it would depend on particular essences even if granted as an accident of absolute essence.
This strengthens the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : أن الوجود إما نفس الماهية أو زائد عليها . فإن كان نفس الماهية والماهيات ليست ببديهية كان الوجود غير بديهي، وإن كان زائداً عليها كان عارضاً لها لأن ذلك معناه فيكون تابعاً للمعروضات في المعقولية إذ لا استقلال للعارض بدون المعروض وهو غير بديهية - فكذا الوجود العارض بل أولى لا يقال الكلام في الوجود المطلق لا في الوجودات الخاصة التي هي العوارض للماهيات ، ولو سلم فالوجود المطلق يكون عارضاً لمطلق الماهية. والكسبيات الماهيات المخصوصة، فعلى تقدير كون الوجود المطلق عارضاً لا يلزم كونه تابعاً للماهيات المكتسبة، لأنا نقول الوجود المطلق عارض للوجودات الخاصة على ما سيجيء فيكون تابعاً لها، وهي تابعة للماهيات المكتسبة، فيكون المطلق (۳) تابعاً لها بالواسطة وهذا معنى زيادة التبعية، وكذا مطلق الماهية عارض للماهيات المخصوصة لكونه صادقاً عليها غير مقوم لها فيكون إنما هي تابعاً لها فيكون الوجود المطلق العارض مطلق الماهية عارضاً لها بالواسطة . والجواب عن الأول : أنا لا نسلم أن العارض يكون تابعاً للمعروض في المعقولية بل ربما يعقل العارض دون المعروض وعدم استقلاله إنما هو في التحقيق في الأعيان ولو سلم فلا نزاع في بداهة بعض الماهيات فيكفي في تعقل الوجود من غير اكتساب لا يقال العارض تابع للمعروض في التحقق حيث ما كان عارضاً، فإن كان في الخارج ففي الخارج، وإن كان في العقل ففي العقل، ء أن زيادة الوجود على الماهية إنما هي في العقل. والمعقول بتبعية الماهية البديهية يكون وجودها الخاص، وليس المطلق ذاتياً له حتى يلزم بداهته بل عارضاً لأنا نقول : ليس معنى العروض في العقل أن لا يتحقق العارض (١) . في العقل بدون المعروض وقائماً به كما في العروض الخارجي، بل إن العقل إذا لاحظهما ولاحظ النسبة بينهما لم يكن المعقول من أحدهما نفس المعقول من الآخر، ولا جزء له بل صادقاً عليه. وسيجيء والوجود المطلق وإن لم يكن ذاتياً للخاص لكنه لازم له بلا نزاع، وليس إلا في العقل إذ لا تمايز في الخارج فتعقل الخاص لا يكون بدون تعقله فيكون بديهياً مثله .
The first: That existence is either the same as essence or additional to it. If it is the same as essence, and essences are not self-evident, then existence is not self-evident; and if it is additional to it, then it is an accident of it, because that is its meaning, so it is dependent upon the subjects in intelligibility, since an accident has no independence without the subject, and it [the subject] is not self-evident. So likewise the accidental existence, rather more so. It cannot be said: The discussion is about absolute existence, not the particular existences that are accidents of essences. And if it is granted, then absolute existence would be an accident of absolute essence. And the acquired are particular essences, so on the assumption that absolute existence is an accident, it does not necessitate its being dependent upon acquired essences, because we say: Absolute existence is an accident of particular existences, as will come, so it is dependent upon them, and they are dependent upon acquired essences, so the absolute (3) is dependent upon them by mediation, and this is the meaning of additional dependency. Likewise, absolute essence is an accident of particular essences, because it is true of them without being constitutive of them, so it is dependent upon them, so absolute existence that is an accident of absolute essence is an accident of them by mediation. And the answer to the first: We do not concede that the accident is dependent upon the subject in intelligibility; rather, the accident may be conceived without the subject, and its lack of independence is only in actual realization in external entities. And if it is granted, then there is no dispute about the self-evidentness of some essences, so it suffices for conceiving existence without acquisition. It cannot be said: The accident is dependent upon the subject in actualization wherever it occurs, so if it is external then in the external, and if it is in the mind then in the mind, since the addition of existence to essence is only in the mind. And what is conceived by following a self-evident essence is its particular existence, and the absolute is not essential to it such that its self-evidentness is necessitated, but rather accidental, because we say: The meaning of accidentality in the mind is not that the accident (1) is not realized in the mind without the subject and subsisting by it as in external accidentality; rather, when the mind observes both and observes the relation between them, the conception of one is not the same as the conception of the other, nor a part of it, but is true of it. And it will come that absolute existence, even if not essential to the particular, is necessary for it without dispute, and it is only in the mind, since there is no distinction in the external, so the conception of the particular cannot be without its conception, so it is self-evident like it.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection (al-awwal) to the self-evidence of existence and its full refutation. The objection runs as a dilemma: existence is either identical to essence or additional to it. If identical, then since essences are not self-evident, existence is not self-evident. If additional, then existence is an accident of essence, and accidents depend on their subjects in intelligibility, so existence cannot be self-evident because it depends on a non-self-evident subject. The objection includes a sub-argument about absolute versus particular existence. The answer (al-jawāb) rejects the premise that mental accident depends on its subject in the same way as external accident. The author argues that an accident can be conceived without its subject in the mind; the dependency is ontological, not conceptual. Even if it is granted that some essences are self-evident, their particular existence is likewise self-evident. The author distinguishes between external and mental accidentality: in the mind, accidentality means the concepts are distinct but not that one cannot be conceived without the other. Absolute existence, though not essential to the particular, is necessary and concept-dependent, so it too is self-evident. The passage thus defends the self-evidentness of existence by redefining the relationship between accident and subject in the mental realm.

Key Terms
الوجود
الماهية
العارض
المعروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That existence's accidentality necessarily implies it is conceived only after the essence is conceived.
Correction: The objection assumes this dependency, but the answer will challenge it by distinguishing mental from external accidentality.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, if existence is additional to essence, what status does it have?
A) It is essential to essence
B) It is an accident of essence
C) It is self-evident
D) It is separate from essence

Key Takeaway
The first objection argues that existence cannot be self-evident because it is either non-self-evident as essence or dependent as an accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own existence, do you find it immediately obvious or do you rely on some reasoning? After reading this objection, are you still certain that your existence is self-evident? How would you defend your certainty against the dilemma presented?
?
This argument claims that existence is either essence or accident, both leading to non-self-evidence. Do you live your life as if your existence is self-evident? What would change in your daily awareness if you seriously doubted the self-evidence of existence?
?
The passage argues that even absolute existence depends on particulars. Reflect on your own concept of 'existence'—do you think of it as something abstract or concrete? How does this affect your understanding of God's existence, which is said to be absolute? Can you apply this reasoning to that? Take a moment to re-evaluate your assumptions.

Refutation of the First Objection: Redefining Accidentality
Refutation differentiating mental and external accidentality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the answer refutes the first objection by distinguishing mental accidentality from external accidentality and re-establishing the self-evidence of existence.

Core Idea
An accident in the mind need not depend on its subject for conception, preserving existence's self-evidence.

The response to the first objection dismantles its key assumption: that an accident requires prior conception of its subject. The answer argues that mental accidentality differs from external accidentality, allowing existence to be self-evident even as an accident. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer's initial rejection
Begin with the phrase 'والجواب عن الأول' and note the direct denial of the objection's premise about accident dependency in intelligibility.
The answer does not accept that accident depends on subject for conception.
2
Understand the mental vs external accident distinction
The answer explains that an accident can be conceived without its subject in the mind; its dependency is only in external reality.
This is the core of the refutation.
3
See the implication for self-evidence
If an accident can be conceived independently, and some essences are self-evident, then their existence can also be self-evident.
The answer uses the example of particular existences.
4
Follow the conclusion about absolute existence
Absolute existence, though not essential, is necessary and conceived with the particular, thus also self-evident.
The answer ends by affirming the self-evidentness of absolute existence.

Disagreement and Self-Evidence: Objection and Refutation
Third objection and its refutation regarding disagreement on self-evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why disagreement about whether something is self-evident does not negate the self-evidence of the judgment itself, and how the refutation preserves the possibility of rational proof for existence.

Core Idea
Disagreement about self-evidence does not disprove the self-evident truth itself.

A common objection against self-evident truths is that if they were truly self-evident, everyone would agree on them. But we know that many supposedly self-evident propositions, like the existence of God, are debated. This passage presents that objection and its incisive refutation, showing that what is debated is not the judgment but its classification as self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the conditional reasoning: if self-evident then no disagreement/argument. Since disagreement exists, conclusion follows.
2
Analyze the answer
The answer draws a distinction: what is not disagreed upon is the self-evident judgment itself, not the opinion that it is self-evident.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this answer preserves the possibility of proving existence even if some deny its self-evidence.
4
Test your understanding
Use the checkpoint question to see if you can apply the distinction correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : أنه لو كان بديهياً لم يختلف العقلاء في بداهته، ولم يفتقر المثبتون منهم إلى الاحتجاج عليها، لكنهم اختلفوا واحتجوا فلم يكن بديهياً . وعن الثالث: أن الذي لا يقع فيه اختلاف العقلاء هو الحكم البديهي
The third: That if it were self-evident, people of intellect would not have differed about its self-evidentness, nor would those affirming it have needed to argue for it; but they differed and argued, so it is not self-evident. And the answer to the third: That that concerning which the people of intellect do not differ is the self-evident judgment.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection (al-thālith) and its answer. The objection claims that if existence were self-evident, there would be no disagreement among rational people about its self-evidentness, and those who affirm it would not need to provide proofs. Since there is disagreement and argumentation, existence cannot be self-evident. The answer restricts the scope of self-evidence: what is beyond disagreement among rational people is the self-evident judgment (al-ḥukm al-badīhī) itself, not the meta-judgment about its self-evidentness. The disagreement is about whether a particular concept is self-evident, not about the concept itself. Thus, the existence of debate does not negate the self-evidence of existence; it only indicates that the status of self-evidence may be hidden to some and requires evidence or attention.

Key Terms
البداهة
الاختلاف
الحكم البديهي
الاحتجاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is truly self-evident, everyone must immediately agree on its truth without any debate.
Correction: Disagreement can occur about whether a proposition is self-evident, without undermining the proposition itself. The self-evident judgment remains true even if some dispute its status.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what is it that people of intellect do not differ about?
A) The concept of existence
B) The self-evident judgment
C) The definitions of existence
D) The arguments for existence

Key Takeaway
Disagreement about whether a proposition is self-evident does not disprove the proposition's self-evidence; it only shows that its status may need to be demonstrated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a truth you hold as self-evident, such as the existence of God. When you encounter a rational person who denies it, does your certainty waver? How does this lesson's distinction between the judgment itself and its self-evident status challenge you to separate your confidence in the truth from your ease in accepting it? Commit to one way you will strengthen your understanding so you can articulate why it is self-evident without relying on it 'just being obvious'.
?
Think of a time you were in a debate about a core Islamic belief and you found yourself repeating 'it's just common sense' or 'it's obvious'. Did that argument hold? What does this lesson teach you about the need to provide reasoning even for what you consider self-evident? Write down one specific belief you will now work to prove rationally, not just assume.
?
The objection in this lesson uses disagreement as a tool to undermine certainty. Have you ever let someone else's doubt cause you to question something you knew was true? How will you apply the refutation—that disagreement is about the claim of self-evidence, not the truth itself—the next time your faith is challenged by another's argument?

Third Objection on Self-Evidence of Existence and Its Answer
Taftazani presents the third objection and answers it by distinguishing conception from judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against the self-evidence of existence and Taftazani's three-part answer distinguishing conception from judgment.

Core Idea
Self-evidence of conception does not imply self-evidence of judgment.

Having addressed earlier objections, Taftazani now tackles a third: if existence were self-evident, why would rational people differ and argue about it? His answer is nuanced, involving the nature of accidents in the mind, the role of nominal definitions, and the crucial distinction between a self-evident conception and a self-evident judgment. This lesson unpacks each part of his response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the opening lines of the Arabic passage where the third objection is stated. The objector argues that disagreement and argument prove existence is not self-evident.
Notice the logical structure of the objection: counterfactual conditional plus observed disagreement.
2
Explore the first answer: accident and substance
Study Taftazani's first response: an accident can be conceived without its substance in the mind. He explains that 'following' in the mind is different from external reality.
Focus on the distinction between conceiving an accident alone versus its independent existence externally.
3
Understand the second answer: definition types
Read the second answer: self-evident concepts are not defined by essential definitions, but can be defined nominally. Definitions of existence are nominal, clarifying the word's meaning.
Nominal definitions do not affect the self-evidence of the concept.
4
Grasp the third answer: conception vs. judgment
Examine the third answer: the conception of existence may be self-evident, but the judgment that it is self-evident is not necessarily so. This judgment may be acquired or hidden, leading to disagreement.
Thus arguments can be reminders, not proofs that existence is not self-evident.
5
Reflect on the whole response
Consider how all three answers work together to preserve the self-evidence of the conception of existence despite apparent disagreement.
This shows the sophistication of Islamic kalam in defending foundational concepts.

الثالث : أنه لو كان بديهياً لم يختلف العقلاء في بداهته، ولم يفتقر المثبتون منهم إلى الاحتجاج عليها، لكنهم اختلفوا واحتجوا فلم يكن بديهياً . والجواب عن الأول : أنا لا نسلم أن العارض يكون تابعاً للمعروض في المعقولية بل ربما يعقل العارض دون المعروض وعدم استقلاله إنما هو في التحقيق في الأعيان ولو سلم فلا نزاع في بداهة بعض الماهيات فيكفي في تعقل الوجود من غير اكتساب لا يقال العارض تابع للمعروض في التحقق حيث ما كان عارضاً، فإن كان في الخارج ففي الخارج، وإن كان في العقل ففي العقل، ء أن زيادة الوجود على الماهية إنما هي في العقل. والمعقول بتبعية الماهية البديهية يكون وجودها الخاص، وليس المطلق ذاتياً له حتى يلزم بداهته بل عارضاً لأنا نقول : ليس معنى العروض في العقل أن لا يتحقق العارض (١) . في العقل بدون المعروض وقائماً به كما في العروض الخارجي، بل إن العقل إذا لاحظهما ولاحظ النسبة بينهما لم يكن المعقول من أحدهما نفس المعقول من الآخر، ولا جزء له بل صادقاً عليه.
وسيجيء
والوجود المطلق وإن لم يكن ذاتياً للخاص لكنه لازم له بلا نزاع، وليس إلا في العقل إذ لا تمايز في الخارج فتعقل الخاص لا يكون بدون تعقله فيكون بديهياً مثله . وعن الثاني : أن البديهي لا يعرف تعريفاً حدياً أو رسمياً لإفادة تصوره لكن قد يعرف تعريفاً اسمياً لإفادة المراد من اللفظ وتصور المعنى من حيث أنه مدلول لفظ وإن كان مت وراً في نفسه، ومن حيث أنه مدلول لفظ آخر، وتعريفات الوجود من هذا القبيل .
وعن الثالث: أن الذي لا يقع فيه اختلاف العقلاء هو الحكم البديهي الواضح ، وبداهة تصور الوجود لا تستلزم بداهة الحكم بأنه بديهي، فيجوز أن يكون هذا الحكم كسبياً أو بديهياً خفياً لا يكون في حكم قولنا : الواحد نصف الاثنين فيقع فيه الاختلاف، ويحتاج على الأول إلى الدليل، وعلى الثاني إلى التنبيه ويكون ما ذكر في معرض الاستدلال تنبيهات
Third: If it were self-evident, then rational people would not differ regarding its self-evidence, and those who affirm it would not need to argue for it. But they have differed and argued, so it is not self-evident. The answer to the first: We do not grant that the accident follows the substance in conceivability; rather, the accident may be conceived without the substance, and its lack of independence is only in actual existence. Even if granted, there is no dispute about the self-evidence of some essences, so the conception of existence without acquisition is sufficient. It cannot be said that the accident follows the substance in actuality wherever it is an accident: if in the external world, then in the external world; if in the mind, then in the mind. But the addition of existence to essence is only in the mind. And what is conceived by following a self-evident essence is its particular existence, not absolute existence, which is not essential to it such that its self-evidence would be necessary; rather it is accidental. For we say: The meaning of accident in the mind is not that the accident does not occur in the mind without the substance and subsisting by it as in external accidentally; rather, when the mind observes both and observes the relation between them, what is conceived of one is not the same as what is conceived of the other, nor a part of it, but true of it. And absolute existence, even if not essential to the particular, is entailed by it without dispute, and it exists only in the mind, since there is no distinction in the external world. Thus, conceiving the particular does not occur without conceiving it, so it is self-evident like it. Answer to the second: The self-evident is not known by a definition of limit or description to convey its conception, but it may be known by a nominal definition to convey what is meant by the word and the conception of the meaning insofar as it is a significate of the word, even if it is already clear in itself, and insofar as it is a significate of another word. Definitions of existence are of this kind. Answer to the third: That in which rational people do not differ is the clear self-evident judgment. The self-evidence of the conception of existence does not entail the self-evidence of the judgment that it is self-evident. It is possible that this judgment is acquired or a hidden self-evident, not like the judgment 'one is half of two', so difference occurs. The first requires evidence, the second requires a reminder, and what is mentioned in the context of argument are reminders.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection against the self-evidence of the conception of existence and its detailed answer. The objector argues that if existence were self-evident, rational people would not disagree about its self-evidence, and those who affirm it would not need to prove it. But they have differed and provided proofs, so it is not self-evident. The author answers this objection by addressing three sub-points. The first sub-point concerns the relationship between an accident and its substance: the opponent claims that an accident cannot be conceived without its substance. The author denies this, asserting that the accident can be conceived independently in the mind, and its lack of independence is only in external reality. Even if granted, some essences are self-evident, and existence can be conceived through them. The author distinguishes between accidental occurrence in the mind versus external reality: in the mind, the accident does not need the substance to be conceived; rather, the mind observes both and sees one as true of the other. Absolute existence is entailed by particular existence and is self-evident because it exists only in the mind. The second sub-point addresses the fact that self-evident concepts are not defined by proper definitions. The author clarifies that they can be defined nominally to indicate what is meant by the word, and definitions of existence are of this type. The third sub-point distinguishes between self-evident conception and self-evident judgment. The conception of existence may be self-evident, but the judgment that it is self-evident may not be; it may be acquired or hidden, requiring evidence or a reminder. Thus, arguments for existence serve as reminders, not proofs of its non-self-evidence.

Key Terms
بديهي
عارض
معروض
وجود
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If existence is self-evident, then the judgment that it is self-evident must also be self-evident.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that self-evidence of conception does not entail self-evidence of the judgment that it is self-evident; the judgment may be acquired or hidden self-evident.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the third objection, what does the self-evidence of the conception of existence not entail?
A) The self-evidence of the judgment that it is self-evident.
B) That existence is an accident.
C) That existence is a substance.
D) That existence is known by definition.

Key Takeaway
A concept can be self-evident even if people argue about its self-evidence, because conception and judgment are distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you disagree with someone about a basic truth like the existence of God, does your frustration stem from assuming their judgment should be as self-evident as your own? How can you separate the self-evidence of the concept from the acquired nature of your judgment about it?
?
Think of a belief you hold that you've never questioned because it seems obviously true. What would it take for you to examine whether that judgment is truly self-evident or a hidden assumption you've acquired?
?
Taftazani says arguments for existence are reminders, not proofs. Who in your life might need a reminder rather than a proof? Are you willing to be patient and treat your own certainty as something that may need reminding as well?
